نسخة شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم من هذه الطبعة كان يملكها بعض أفاضل مليبار»، ثم انتقل ملكها إلى أخينا الفاضل فضيلة الشيخ عبد الواسع الباقوي بن القاضي عبد الله الباقوي الكُتفرَمِي المليباري رحمة الله ، تكرم علي بهذه النسخة العتيقة حيث أعارنيها، مد الله في عمره خادما للعلم وأهله، وجزاه عني وعن الإسلام خيرا. (۲) متن السلم المطبوع في آخر شرح القاضي مبارك، طبع بالمطبعة المصطفائية ، الهند ، عام ١٢٩٦هـ ، وهي أيضا غير كاملة؛ حيث تنتهي عند التصورات. وبهامش السلم في هذه الطبعة حاشية المصنف عليه، المعروف أحيانا بالمنهية . وأشير إلى هذه النسخة بـ«السلم». (۳) شرح بحر العلوم الذي سأذكر بياناته . (٤) شرح حمد الله على السلم طبعة مطبع علوي، لكهنو الهند، عام ۱۳۱۳هـ، وبهامشه حاشية محمد عبد الحق بن فضل الحق الخيرابادي الحنفي، وهذه الطبعة تبدأ من التصديقات، وليس بها قسم التصورات. وأشير إلى هذه النسخة بـ«حمد الله». (٥) شرح بحر العلوم وهو الكتاب الذي بأيدينا الآن، وهو شرح ممزوج كامل يغطي جميع السلم، اعتمدت على طبعة له، طبعت بمطبع مجتبائي دلهي الهند، عام ١٣٣٠هـ، وتقع في ٢٧٦ صفحة، طبع الحجر، باهتمام المولوي حافظ سيد محمد عبد الأحد، وفي هامشها تعليقات للأستاذين: خليل أحمد سنبهلي، ومحمد إلياس بشاوري، وبهامشها أيضا منهيات الشارح بحر العلوم على شرحه وقد اعتمدت عليه أيضا في تحقيق شرح بحر العلوم، وقارنت هذه الطبعة بما نقله العلامة العطار من بحر العلوم في حاشيته على الخبيصي، وهو ليس بالقليل، فإن وجدت اختلافا بين هذه النسخة وبين نقل العطار سجلت ذلك أيضا في الهامش. وأشير إليه بـ«بحر العلوم». تعريف بكتاب شرح سلم العلوم هذا، وقد ورد في معجم المطبوعات العربية في شبه القارة الهندية الباكستانية أنه طبع في المطبعة المذكورة مع تعلقات الأستاذين المذكورين ، ولكن في عام ١٣٠٩هـ / ۱۸۹۲م) ، ولكني لا أعرف هل هي طبعة أخرى مختلفة عما اعتمدت عليه ـ فيكون الكتاب قد طبع مرتين على الأقل ـ أم أنه أخطأ بدليل أنه لم يذكر في معجمه غير هذه الطبعة التي أشار إليها، ولم يشر إلى طبعة عام ۱۳۳۰هـ ، التي حصلت عليها واعتمدت عليها، وهذا مخالف في الغالب، والله أعلم. وقد حصلت على صورة من هذه الطبعة التي أشرت إليها عن طريق الأستاذ الفاضل الشيخ عبد الله الأحسني بن العلامة الشيخ سليمان بن حفظهما الله ـ وهو من أعضاء هيئة التدريس في كلية إحياء السنة الإسلامية في بلدة «أُدُكُنْغَلْ» في مقاطعة «مالا فرم» مليبار الهند، حيث صورها لي مشكورا ، فالله أسأل أن يكافئه ويجعل ذلك في ميزان حسناته . وجعلت الفروق ـ في الغالب بين القوسين هكذا [ ] ، وقليلا ما تركت ذلك، وفي الحالتين جميعا أشرتُ إلى بيان الفروق في الهامش، وإذا اتضح لي أو غلب على ظني أن نسخة ما خطاً علميا نبهت على ذلك، وقلتُ إن النسخة الفلانية صواب والأخرى خطأ ، وذكرتُ وجه ذلك، وأشرت إلى ما يترتب على بعض الاختلافات من الفوائد المعنوية، مثل ما فعلت في مبحث تقسيم الدلالة إلى مطابقية وتضمنية والتزامية؛ حيث اختلفت نسخ السلم، وترتبت على الاختلاف فائدة معنوية، وكذا غير ذلك من المواضع التي يطلع عليها القارئ عند القراءة.
A copy of Sharh Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum from this edition was owned by some virtuous people of Malabar, then its ownership transferred to our virtuous brother, the venerable Shaykh Abd al-Wasi' al-Baquwi son of Qadi Abd Allah al-Baquwi al-Kutaframi al-Malibari, may God have mercy on him. He honored me with this ancient copy by lending it to me. May God prolong his life as a servant of knowledge and its people, and reward him on my behalf and on behalf of Islam. (2) The text of al-Sullam printed at the end of Sharh al-Qadi Mubarak, printed at al-Mustafaiyya Press, India, in the year 1296 AH, and it is also incomplete, ending at the tasawwurat. In the margin of al-Sullam in this edition is the author's gloss on it, sometimes known as al-Munhiyya. I refer to this copy as 'al-Sullam'. (3) Sharh Bahr al-Ulum, which I will mention its details. (4) Sharh Hamd Allah on al-Sullam, printed at Matba' Alawi, Lucknow, India, in the year 1313 AH, and in its margin is a gloss by Muhammad Abd al-Haqq ibn Fadl al-Haqq al-Khayrabadi al-Hanafi. This edition begins from the tasdiqat and does not contain the section on tasawwurat. I refer to this copy as 'Hamd Allah'. (5) Sharh Bahr al-Ulum, which is the book before us now. It is a complete mixed commentary covering all of al-Sullam. I relied on an edition of it printed at Maktab Mujtabai Delhi, India, in the year 1330 AH, consisting of 276 pages, lithograph, under the care of al-Mawlawi Hafiz Sayyid Muhammad Abd al-Ahad. In its margin are annotations by the two professors: Khalil Ahmad Sanbhali and Muhammad Ilyas Peshawari, and in its margin also are the munhiyat of the commentator Bahr al-Ulum on his commentary. I also relied on it in editing Sharh Bahr al-Ulum, and I compared this edition with what al-Allama al-Attar transmitted from Bahr al-Ulum in his gloss on al-Khabisi, which is not insignificant. If I found a difference between this copy and al-Attar's transmission, I also recorded that in the margin. I refer to it as 'Bahr al-Ulum'. Introduction to the book Sharh Sullam al-Ulum: This has been mentioned in the Dictionary of Arabic Publications in the Indo-Pakistani Subcontinent that it was printed in the aforementioned press with the annotations of the two mentioned professors, but in the year 1309 AH / 1892 CE. However, I do not know whether it is a different edition from the one I relied on — thus the book would have been printed at least twice — or if he was mistaken, because he did not mention in his dictionary any edition other than the one he referred to, and he did not refer to the 1330 AH edition that I obtained and relied on. This is generally contradictory, and God knows best. I obtained a copy of this edition that I referred to through the virtuous professor Shaykh Abd Allah al-Ahsani ibn al-Allama Shaykh Sulayman ibn, may God preserve them — who is a faculty member at the College of Revival of the Islamic Sunnah in the town of 'Odungall' in Malappuram district, Malabar, India, who kindly photographed it for me. I ask God to reward him and place that in the balance of his good deeds. I placed the differences mostly between brackets like this [ ], and rarely did I omit that. In both cases I referred to explaining the differences in the margin. If it became clear to me or I strongly suspected that a copy had a scholarly error, I pointed it out and said that such and such copy is correct and the other is wrong, and I mentioned the reason for that, and I referred to what follows from some differences in terms of meaningful benefits, such as I did in the section on the division of signification into conformity, inclusion, and implication, where the copies of al-Sullam differed, and a meaningful benefit resulted from the difference, and likewise other places that the reader will come across when reading.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction by the editor of the printed edition of 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum'. It describes the various manuscripts and printed editions he used to establish the text. The editor begins by mentioning a manuscript copy of the commentary that he borrowed from a scholar. He then lists other sources: (1) the printed text of 'al-Sullam' (the matn) found at the end of 'Sharh al-Qadi Mubarak', which is incomplete, ending at the section on tasawwurat (conceptions); (2) 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum' itself, whose details he will provide; (3) 'Sharh Hamd Allah' on al-Sullam, which starts from tasdiqat (assents) and lacks the tasawwurat section; and (4) the primary copy of 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum' that he uses, a lithograph edition from 1330 AH. He also mentions that he compared this edition with quotations from al-Attar's gloss. He explains his methodology for indicating textual variants: he places differences in brackets and notes them in the margin, and if he determines an error, he states which reading is correct and why. He gives an example from the section on the division of signification (dalala) into conformity (mutabiqa), inclusion (tadammun), and implication (iltizam), where variant readings led to a meaningful scholarly benefit. The passage highlights the editor's careful critical approach.

شرح

Editorial Methodology for Critical Editions in Islamic Logic
Editor explains his use of brackets and marginal notes for variants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the editor's method for noting variant readings in a critical edition of an Islamic logic text.

Core Idea
Critical editions of Islamic texts preserve scholarly integrity by transparently noting variant readings.

This passage introduces the editor's methodology for Sharh Sullam al-Ulum, showing how he handled differences between manuscript copies. Understanding this process is essential for scholars who need to evaluate the reliability of a text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the notation method
The editor places variant readings in brackets [ ] and explains them in the margin. This makes the differences visible and traceable.
2
Recognize the decision process
When one version is clearly erroneous (scientific error), the editor states which is correct and justifies it. He also notes meaningful benefits from certain differences.
3
Analyze the specific example
The editor mentions the division of signification (dalalah) into correspondence, implication, and commitment. The editions of al-Sullam differed here, and the editor's method highlights the benefit of the variant.

تعريف بكتاب شرح سلم العلوم

هذا، وقد ورد في معجم المطبوعات العربية في شبه القارة الهندية الباكستانية أنه طبع في المطبعة المذكورة مع تعلقات الأستاذين المذكورين ، ولكن في عام ١٣٠٩هـ / ۱۸۹۲م) (۱) ، ولكني لا أعرف هل هي طبعة أخرى مختلفة عما اعتمدت عليه ـ فيكون الكتاب قد طبع مرتين على الأقل ـ أم أنه أخطأ بدليل أنه لم يذكر في معجمه غير هذه الطبعة التي أشار إليها، ولم يشر إلى طبعة عام ۱۳۳۰هـ ، التي حصلت عليها واعتمدت عليها، وهذا مخالف في الغالب، والله أعلم.

شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
وختاما - وإن كان لا ختام لمثل هذا الموضوع ـ لم يكن هذا أمرا سهلا
بالمرة، بل كان عملا شاقا، شغل القدر الكثير من وقتي وجهدي، أسأل الله الكريم أن ينفع به الكاتب والقارئ والمعلم والطالب.
Introduction to the book Sharh Sullam al-Ulum. This: It has been mentioned in the Dictionary of Arabic Publications in the Indo-Pakistani Subcontinent that it was printed in the mentioned press with the commentaries of the two mentioned professors, but in the year 1309 AH / 1892 CE (1). But I do not know if it is another edition different from what I relied upon — such that the book was printed at least twice — or that he was mistaken, evidenced by the fact that he mentioned in his dictionary no other edition than this one he referred to, and he did not refer to the edition of 1330 AH, which I obtained and relied upon. This is mostly contradictory, and Allah knows best. According to his habit, Ahmad. I obtained a copy of this edition I referred to through the virtuous teacher Sheikh Abdullah al-Ahsani ibn al-Allamah Sheikh Sulayman, may Allah preserve them both — he is a faculty member at the College for the Revival of the Prophetic Sunnah in the town of 'Udung'angal in the Malabar district of India. He photographed it for me, may he be thanked. I ask Allah the Generous to reward him and place it in the scale of his good deeds. I placed the differences — mostly in brackets like this [ ], and rarely did I omit that, and in both cases I referred to the explanation of the differences in the margin. If it became clear to me or I predominantly thought that a version was a scientific error, I alerted to that, said such-and-such version is correct and the other is wrong, and mentioned the reason for that. I referred to the meaningful benefits that result from some differences, such as what I did in the section on the division of signification into correspondence, implication, and commitment; where the editions of al-Sullam differed, and a meaningful benefit resulted from the difference. And likewise other places that the reader will come across when reading. (1) Dictionary of Arabic Publications in the Indo-Pakistani Subcontinent, Dr. Ahmad Khan: 291. Sharh Bahr al-Ulum 'ala Sullam al-Ulum. And finally — even though there is no finality for such a topic — this was not at all an easy matter, but rather an arduous task that consumed much of my time and effort. I ask Allah the Generous to benefit through it the writer, reader, teacher, and student.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is the front matter of the book 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum 'ala Sullam al-Ulum', where the editor introduces the edition. He begins by noting a discrepancy in the bibliography regarding the printing date of the book: the Dictionary of Arabic Publications in the Indo-Pakistani Subcontinent states it was printed in 1309 AH, but the editor has a copy from 1330 AH. He is unsure whether this indicates two separate editions or an error in the dictionary. He then thanks Sheikh Abdullah al-Ahsani for providing a copy of the 1309 edition. The editor explains his editorial method: he encloses variant readings in brackets [ ] and notes them in the margin. If one version is clearly erroneous, he points out the correct reading and justifies it. He highlights a specific case in the section on the division of signification (al-dalalah) into correspondence (mutabaqah), implication (tadammun), and commitment (iltizam), where editions of the original text (al-Sullam) differed, and a meaningful benefit arises from the difference. He concludes by acknowledging the difficulty of the task and praying for benefit. This is not a logical argument but a methodological preface, useful for understanding how the edition was prepared.

Concluding an Introduction: Humility and Prayer in Islamic Scholarship
Author concludes introduction, notes difficulty, prays for benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rhetorical components of a classical scholar's concluding remarks and explain their purpose within the Islamic scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
A scholar's conclusion is not an end, but a humble invocation for benefit.

The passage you will read is the closing of the introduction to Bahr Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-'Ulum. The author, after presenting his work, does not boast but instead acknowledges the difficulty and prays for the readers and students. This humility is a hallmark of Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Notice the opening phrase
Read 'وختاما - وإن كان لا ختام لمثل هذا الموضوع'. The author says 'finally' but immediately qualifies that there is no true end for such a subject. What does this reveal about his view of the subject matter?
This shows his recognition of the depth and scope of logic.
2
Identify the description of his effort
Find the phrase 'لم يكن هذا أمرا سهلا بالمرة، بل كان عملا شاقا'. How does describing his work as strenuous and time-consuming frame his authority?
It establishes his credibility as someone who labored deeply.
3
Find the prayer
Locate 'أسأل الله الكريم أن ينفع به الكاتب والقارئ والمعلم والطالب'. Why does he include all these roles?
He prays for benefit to everyone connected with the work, reflecting a sense of communal scholarship.
4
Consider the transition clause
Look at the fragment on page 100: 'من الأصول المستعان بها'. How does this connect to the next section?
It likely introduces the principles (usul) that will be discussed next.

وختاما - وإن كان لا ختام لمثل هذا الموضوع ـ لم يكن هذا أمرا سهلا بالمرة، بل كان عملا شاقا، شغل القدر الكثير من وقتي وجهدي، أسأل الله الكريم أن ينفع به الكاتب والقارئ والمعلم والطالب. ر الأُصُولِ المُسْتَعَانِ بِهَا

Commentary
This passage is the author's closing statement from the introduction of Bahr Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-'Ulum. He begins by acknowledging that a subject like logic has no true end, yet he must conclude. He then describes the difficulty of his work, calling it a strenuous task that consumed much of his time and energy. Finally, he offers a prayer to Allah that the work benefit all who engage with it: the writer, reader, teacher, and student. The fragment on page 100 likely introduces the next section on the principles (usul) relied upon, but the text is cut off.

Introduction to Mulla Hasan's Commentary
Title page introducing the commentary by Mulla Hasan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a sharh title page and explain its role in the transmission of Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A commentary (sharh) unlocks the condensed knowledge of the matn.

This passage marks the beginning of Mulla Hasan's commentary on Bahr Ulum. While it contains no logical argument, it introduces a key genre: the sharh. Understanding what a sharh is and how to approach it is essential for navigating classical Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the text type
Recognize that this is a title page, not the main text. Look for keywords like 'شرح' to identify it as a commentary.
2
Distinguish commentary from base text
Understand that the sharh explains and expands upon the matn. The title page usually names both the commentator and the original work.
3
Note the author and work
Here, the commentator is al-Mulla Hasan, and the base text is Bahr Ulum. This helps contextualize the logical content that follows.
4
Set expectation for content
A commentary typically clarifies difficult passages, provides examples, and may critique or defend positions. Prepare for deeper analysis.

Reading a Title Page: Sullat al-'Ulum and Its Author
Title page introducing the book and author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author, title, and subject of Sullat al-'Ulum and connect him to his work in Usul al-Fiqh.

Core Idea
A title page is a scholarly fingerprint: author, title, and significance.

Before diving into logic, we must know our source. This title page introduces the book Sullat al-'Ulum by Imam Muhiibb Allah al-Bihari, a Hanafi scholar also famous for Musallam al-Thubut in Usul al-Fiqh. Understanding the author's background helps us appreciate the text's approach.

Guided Path
1
Identify the title and subject
Look for the title of the work. The title 'Sullat al-'Ulum' is followed by parentheses indicating its subject: 'fi Ilm al-Mantiq' (on the science of logic).
The title means 'Basket of Sciences', suggesting a compendium.
2
Identify the author and his full name
Find the author's name: 'al-Imam al-Shaykh Muhiibb Allah ibn Abd al-Shakur al-'Uthmani al-Hanafi al-Bihari al-Hindi'. Note the nisbas that indicate his school (Hanafi) and origin (India).
The honorific 'Imam' indicates high scholarly status.
3
Note the author's other major work
The author is also described as 'sahib Musallam al-Thubut', a well-known work on Usul al-Fiqh. This tells us he is a specialist in legal theory.
Musallam al-Thubut is a standard Hanafi usul text.
4
Identify the editor and his credentials
The title page also mentions the editor 'Abd al-Tasir Ahmad al-Shafi'i al-Malibari', with a Master's degree in Creed and Philosophy from Al-Azhar.
The editor's credentials lend authority to this edition.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this information matters: the author's background in usul al-fiqh shapes his logical approach, and the editor's Azhar training ensures scholarly rigour.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

The Purpose of Logic: The Scale of the Sciences
Author opens the matn with praise, introduces the treatise, and defines knowledge as conception or assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the role of logic in Islamic sciences using the metaphor of the scale.

Core Idea
Logic is the scale that weighs arguments, protecting truth in Islamic disciplines.

This opening passage of Sullam al-'Ulum immediately establishes logic as the 'art of the scale' (sina'at al-mizan). The author frames his work as a ladder to the sciences, and his praise of Allah and the Prophet uses logical terms like 'premises of the religion'. Understanding this front matter is crucial to appreciating how logic serves as a foundational tool for every Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Praise
Notice the author's praise of Allah: 'He is not delimited, nor conceived, neither produces nor changes.' These negations are logical terms; why does he use them to describe Allah?
2
Analyze the Scale Metaphor
The author calls this treatise on the 'art of the scale'. In logic, a scale weighs arguments—separating valid from invalid. This metaphor permeates the tradition.
3
Connect to the 'Ladder of Sciences'
The title 'Sullam al-'Ulum' means ladder. Logic is the ladder that ascends to all sciences. Why is it foundational for acquiring knowledge in every field?
4
Recognize the Companions as 'Premises'
The companions are called 'muqaddimat al-din'—premises of the religion. A logical premise is the foundation of a proof. How does this highlight their role in transmitting the faith?

متن سلم العلوم
الله الرحمن الرحيم
سُبْحَانَهُ ، مَا أَعْظَمَ شَانَه ، لاَ يُحَدُّ، وَلا يُتَصَوَّرُ، لَا يُنْتِجُ ، وَلَا يَتَغَيَّرُ ،
تَعَالَى عَنِ الْجِنْسِ وَالْجِهَاتِ، جَعَلَ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ وَالْجُزْنِيَّاتِ الْإِيمَانُ بِهِ نِعْمَ التَّصْدِيقُ ، وَالاِعْتِصَامُ بِهِ حَبَّذَا التَّوْفِيقُ ، وَالصَّلاَةُ وَالسَّلَامُ عَلَى مَنْ بُعِثَ بِالدَّلِيل الَّذِي فِيهِ شِفَاءٌ لِكُلِّ عَلِيلٍ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَأَصْحَابِهِ الَّذِينَ هُمْ مُقَدِّمَاتُ الدِّينِ، حُجَجُ الْهِدَايَةِ وَالْيَقِينِ ، أَمَّا بَعْدُ ،
فَهَذِهِ رِسَالَةٌ فِي صِنَاعَةِ الْمِيزَانِ ، سَمَّيْتُهَا بِـ سُلَّم الْعُلُومِ» ، اللَّهُمَّ اجْعَلْهُ بَيْنَ الْمُتُونِ كَالشَّمْسِ بَيْنَ النُّجُومِ.
مُقَدِّمَةٌ : الْعِلْمُ التَّصَوُّرُ، وَهُوَ الْحَاضِرُ عِنْدَ الْمُدْرِكِ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ مِنْ أَجْلَى الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ ، كَالنُّورِ وَالسُّرُورِ ، نَعَمْ ، تَنْقِيحُ حَقِيقَتِهِ عَسِيرٌ جِدًّا، فَإِنْ كَانَ اعْتِقَادًا لِنِسْبَةٍ خَبَرِيَّةٍ فَتَصْدِيقٌ وَحُكْمْ، وَإِلا فَتَصَوُّرٌ سَاذَجُ ، وَهُمَا نَوْعَانِ مُتَبَابِنَانِ مِنَ الْإِدْرَاكِ ضَرُورَةٌ ، نَعَمْ، لاَ حَجْرَ فِي التَّصَوُّرِ ، فَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ . وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ وَالْمَعْلُومَ مُتَّحِدَانِ بِالذَّاتِ، فَإِذَا تَصَوَّرْنَا التَّصْدِيقَ فَهُمَا وَاحِدٌ ، وَقَدْ قُلْتُمْ إِنَّهُمَا مُتَخَالِفَانِ حَقِيقَةٌ. وَحَلُهُ ـ عَلَى مَا
Matn Sullam al-'Ulum. In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Glory be to Him, how great is His status! He is not delimited, nor is He conceived, He neither produces nor changes. Exalted above genus and directions. He made the universals and particulars. Belief in Him is an excellent assent, and adherence to Him is a blessed success. Prayers and peace be upon the one sent with the proof that is a cure for every ill, and upon his family and companions who are the premises of the religion, the proofs of guidance and certainty. To proceed: This is a treatise on the art of the scale, which I have named 'Sullam al-'Ulum'. O Allah, make it among the texts like the sun among the stars. Introduction: Knowledge is conception, and it is that which is present to the perceiver. The truth is that it is among the most evident of axiomatic things, like light and joy. Yes, refining its true nature is very difficult. If it is a belief in a predicative relation, then it is assent and judgment; otherwise it is bare conception. They are two mutually opposite types of perception, necessarily. Yes, there is no restriction on conception, so it relates to everything. Here there is a famous doubt, which is: knowledge and the known are identical in essence. So if we conceive of assent, they are one, and you have said that they are different in reality. Its solution, according to what...
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the matn 'Sullam al-'Ulum' by al-Akhdari. It begins with the basmalah and a praise of Allah, highlighting His transcendence beyond delimitation and genus. The author then sends blessings on the Prophet and his companions, referring to them as 'premises of the religion'—a logical term emphasizing their foundational role. The author states that this treatise is on the art of the scale (logic), naming it 'Sullam al-'Ulum' (The Ladder of Sciences) and prays for its acceptance. The introduction (muqaddima) then defines knowledge (al-'ilm) as conception (tasawwur), which is the presence of the object's form in the mind. The author notes that although the conception is self-evident, clarifying its precise nature is difficult. He then distinguishes between tasawwur and tasdiq: if the mental content involves a belief in a predicative relation, it is assent (tasdiq) and judgment; otherwise, it is bare conception. These are two necessarily distinct types of perception. Conception is unrestricted and can relate to anything. Finally, the passage presents a famous doubt: if knowledge and the known are identical in essence, then when we conceive of assent, they would be one, contradicting the claim that they are different in reality. The solution is promised but cut off. This sets the stage for a key epistemological debate in logic.

Key Terms
العلم (al-'ilm): Knowledge, defined broadly as either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). It is the presence of something in the mind. [Note: In later texts, al-'ilm is often defined more narrowly as certain knowledge; here it includes both conception and assent.]
التصوّر (al-tasawwur): Conception: the simple presence of a form in the mind without any judgment or affirmation of a relation. [Note: Stable term across the tradition; sometimes contrasted with tasawwur as imaginal vs. conceptual.]
التصديق (al-tasdiq): Assent: a belief in a predicative relation, which includes a judgment (hukm). [Note: Generally stable; in some post-Avicennan logic, tasdiq is considered the true form of knowledge.]
المعلوم (al-ma'lum): The known object; the thing that is known, which the doubt claims is identical with the knowledge. [Note: In Islamic epistemology, the identity of knowledge and known is debated; this text raises the issue.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Logic is a ladder that helps one ascend to all sciences.
B) The book is very long and has many steps.
C) It is a collection of poems about the sciences.
D) It is a translation of a Greek work.

Key Takeaway
Logic is the art of the scale and the ladder of sciences, essential for sound reasoning in every Islamic discipline.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
6 min
Defining Knowledge: Conception vs. Assent
Author opens the matn with praise, introduces the treatise, and defines knowledge as conception or assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conception (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq) as two types of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either bare conception or judgment-laden assent; these two are fundamentally distinct.

The introduction of Sullam al-'Ulum defines knowledge (al-'ilm) in two categories: conception and assent. This binary classification is the backbone of Islamic logic. The passage stresses that they are 'mutabayanani'—opposite types of perception. Recognizing the difference is essential for understanding how definitions and syllogisms work.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Definition of 'Ilm
The passage begins the introduction with 'al-'ilm al-tasawwur'—knowledge is conception. But then it qualifies: if it involves a belief in a relation, it is assent. So knowledge comprises two types.
2
Identify the Criteria for Tasdiq
Tasdiq is defined as 'i'tiqad li-nisba khabariyya'—a belief in a predicative relation. This includes a judgment. Contrast this with tasawwur, which is simply the presence of a form.
3
Note the Author's Emphasis on Clarity
The author says tasawwur is among the most evident axiomatic things (ajla al-badihiyyat), yet refining its nature is difficult. This shows that self-evident concepts still require careful analysis.
4
Understand the Mutual Exclusivity
The two types are 'mutabayanani'—necessarily distinct. This means a mental content cannot be both a bare conception and an assent simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن سلم العلوم
الله الرحمن الرحيم
سُبْحَانَهُ ، مَا أَعْظَمَ شَانَه ، لاَ يُحَدُّ، وَلا يُتَصَوَّرُ، لَا يُنْتِجُ ، وَلَا يَتَغَيَّرُ ،
تَعَالَى عَنِ الْجِنْسِ وَالْجِهَاتِ، جَعَلَ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ وَالْجُزْنِيَّاتِ الْإِيمَانُ بِهِ نِعْمَ التَّصْدِيقُ ، وَالاِعْتِصَامُ بِهِ حَبَّذَا التَّوْفِيقُ ، وَالصَّلاَةُ وَالسَّلَامُ عَلَى مَنْ بُعِثَ بِالدَّلِيل الَّذِي فِيهِ شِفَاءٌ لِكُلِّ عَلِيلٍ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَأَصْحَابِهِ الَّذِينَ هُمْ مُقَدِّمَاتُ الدِّينِ، حُجَجُ الْهِدَايَةِ وَالْيَقِينِ ، أَمَّا بَعْدُ ،
فَهَذِهِ رِسَالَةٌ فِي صِنَاعَةِ الْمِيزَانِ ، سَمَّيْتُهَا بِـ سُلَّم الْعُلُومِ» ، اللَّهُمَّ اجْعَلْهُ بَيْنَ الْمُتُونِ كَالشَّمْسِ بَيْنَ النُّجُومِ.
مُقَدِّمَةٌ : الْعِلْمُ التَّصَوُّرُ، وَهُوَ الْحَاضِرُ عِنْدَ الْمُدْرِكِ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ مِنْ أَجْلَى الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ ، كَالنُّورِ وَالسُّرُورِ ، نَعَمْ ، تَنْقِيحُ حَقِيقَتِهِ عَسِيرٌ جِدًّا، فَإِنْ كَانَ اعْتِقَادًا لِنِسْبَةٍ خَبَرِيَّةٍ فَتَصْدِيقٌ وَحُكْمْ، وَإِلا فَتَصَوُّرٌ سَاذَجُ ، وَهُمَا نَوْعَانِ مُتَبَابِنَانِ مِنَ الْإِدْرَاكِ ضَرُورَةٌ ، نَعَمْ، لاَ حَجْرَ فِي التَّصَوُّرِ ، فَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ . وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ وَالْمَعْلُومَ مُتَّحِدَانِ بِالذَّاتِ، فَإِذَا تَصَوَّرْنَا التَّصْدِيقَ فَهُمَا وَاحِدٌ ، وَقَدْ قُلْتُمْ إِنَّهُمَا مُتَخَالِفَانِ حَقِيقَةٌ. وَحَلُهُ ـ عَلَى مَا

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
The Famous Doubt: Are Knowledge and the Known Identical?
Author opens the matn with praise, introduces the treatise, and defines knowledge as conception or assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the doubt about the identity of knowledge and the known and its implications for the conception-assent distinction.

Core Idea
If knowledge and the known are identical, then our knowledge of assent would itself be assent—a problem that threatens the bipartite division.

After defining knowledge as conception and assent, the author raises a famous doubt: if knowledge and the known are identical in essence, then when we conceive of assent, that conception would itself be assent, collapsing the distinction. This puzzle has occupied logicians for centuries. The passage presents it but the solution is cut off, inviting the reader to engage with the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Doubt
The doubt claims that knowledge and the known are one in essence. Therefore, any knowledge of a thing is identical to the thing itself.
2
Apply to the Two Types
If I conceive of assent, then according to the doubt, my conception is identical to the assent itself. But the author said conception and assent are different. This creates a contradiction.
3
Identify the Tension
The author had previously stipulated that tasawwur and tasdiq are 'mutabayanani' (mutually exclusive). The doubt challenges this exclusivity.
4
Consider the Missing Solution
The text says 'its solution, according to what...' and cuts off. Think about possible solutions: perhaps the identity only holds for God's knowledge, or for mental existence vs. external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن سلم العلوم
الله الرحمن الرحيم
سُبْحَانَهُ ، مَا أَعْظَمَ شَانَه ، لاَ يُحَدُّ، وَلا يُتَصَوَّرُ، لَا يُنْتِجُ ، وَلَا يَتَغَيَّرُ ،
تَعَالَى عَنِ الْجِنْسِ وَالْجِهَاتِ، جَعَلَ الْكُلِّيَّاتِ وَالْجُزْنِيَّاتِ الْإِيمَانُ بِهِ نِعْمَ التَّصْدِيقُ ، وَالاِعْتِصَامُ بِهِ حَبَّذَا التَّوْفِيقُ ، وَالصَّلاَةُ وَالسَّلَامُ عَلَى مَنْ بُعِثَ بِالدَّلِيل الَّذِي فِيهِ شِفَاءٌ لِكُلِّ عَلِيلٍ، وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَأَصْحَابِهِ الَّذِينَ هُمْ مُقَدِّمَاتُ الدِّينِ، حُجَجُ الْهِدَايَةِ وَالْيَقِينِ ، أَمَّا بَعْدُ ،
فَهَذِهِ رِسَالَةٌ فِي صِنَاعَةِ الْمِيزَانِ ، سَمَّيْتُهَا بِـ سُلَّم الْعُلُومِ» ، اللَّهُمَّ اجْعَلْهُ بَيْنَ الْمُتُونِ كَالشَّمْسِ بَيْنَ النُّجُومِ.
مُقَدِّمَةٌ : الْعِلْمُ التَّصَوُّرُ، وَهُوَ الْحَاضِرُ عِنْدَ الْمُدْرِكِ، وَالْحَقُّ أَنَّهُ مِنْ أَجْلَى الْبَدِيهِيَّاتِ ، كَالنُّورِ وَالسُّرُورِ ، نَعَمْ ، تَنْقِيحُ حَقِيقَتِهِ عَسِيرٌ جِدًّا، فَإِنْ كَانَ اعْتِقَادًا لِنِسْبَةٍ خَبَرِيَّةٍ فَتَصْدِيقٌ وَحُكْمْ، وَإِلا فَتَصَوُّرٌ سَاذَجُ ، وَهُمَا نَوْعَانِ مُتَبَابِنَانِ مِنَ الْإِدْرَاكِ ضَرُورَةٌ ، نَعَمْ، لاَ حَجْرَ فِي التَّصَوُّرِ ، فَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ . وَهَهُنَا شَكٍّ مَشْهُورٌ ، وَهُوَ: أَنَّ الْعِلْمَ وَالْمَعْلُومَ مُتَّحِدَانِ بِالذَّاتِ، فَإِذَا تَصَوَّرْنَا التَّصْدِيقَ فَهُمَا وَاحِدٌ ، وَقَدْ قُلْتُمْ إِنَّهُمَا مُتَخَالِفَانِ حَقِيقَةٌ. وَحَلُهُ ـ عَلَى مَا
Matn Sullam al-'Ulum. In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Glory be to Him, how great is His status! He is not delimited, nor is He conceived, He neither produces nor changes. Exalted above genus and directions. He made the universals and particulars. Belief in Him is an excellent assent, and adherence to Him is a blessed success. Prayers and peace be upon the one sent with the proof that is a cure for every ill, and upon his family and companions who are the premises of the religion, the proofs of guidance and certainty. To proceed: This is a treatise on the art of the scale, which I have named 'Sullam al-'Ulum'. O Allah, make it among the texts like the sun among the stars. Introduction: Knowledge is conception, and it is that which is present to the perceiver. The truth is that it is among the most evident of axiomatic things, like light and joy. Yes, refining its true nature is very difficult. If it is a belief in a predicative relation, then it is assent and judgment; otherwise it is bare conception. They are two mutually opposite types of perception, necessarily. Yes, there is no restriction on conception, so it relates to everything. Here there is a famous doubt, which is: knowledge and the known are identical in essence. So if we conceive of assent, they are one, and you have said that they are different in reality. Its solution, according to what...
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the matn 'Sullam al-'Ulum' by al-Akhdari. It begins with the basmalah and a praise of Allah, highlighting His transcendence beyond delimitation and genus. The author then sends blessings on the Prophet and his companions, referring to them as 'premises of the religion'—a logical term emphasizing their foundational role. The author states that this treatise is on the art of the scale (logic), naming it 'Sullam al-'Ulum' (The Ladder of Sciences) and prays for its acceptance. The introduction (muqaddima) then defines knowledge (al-'ilm) as conception (tasawwur), which is the presence of the object's form in the mind. The author notes that although the conception is self-evident, clarifying its precise nature is difficult. He then distinguishes between tasawwur and tasdiq: if the mental content involves a belief in a predicative relation, it is assent (tasdiq) and judgment; otherwise, it is bare conception. These are two necessarily distinct types of perception. Conception is unrestricted and can relate to anything. Finally, the passage presents a famous doubt: if knowledge and the known are identical in essence, then when we conceive of assent, they would be one, contradicting the claim that they are different in reality. The solution is promised but cut off. This sets the stage for a key epistemological debate in logic.

Key Terms
العلم (al-'ilm): Knowledge, defined broadly as either conception (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). It is the presence of something in the mind. [Note: In later texts, al-'ilm is often defined more narrowly as certain knowledge; here it includes both conception and assent.]
التصوّر (al-tasawwur): Conception: the simple presence of a form in the mind without any judgment or affirmation of a relation. [Note: Stable term across the tradition; sometimes contrasted with tasawwur as imaginal vs. conceptual.]
التصديق (al-tasdiq): Assent: a belief in a predicative relation, which includes a judgment (hukm). [Note: Generally stable; in some post-Avicennan logic, tasdiq is considered the true form of knowledge.]
المعلوم (al-ma'lum): The known object; the thing that is known, which the doubt claims is identical with the knowledge. [Note: In Islamic epistemology, the identity of knowledge and known is debated; this text raises the issue.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the 'famous doubt' presented in this passage?
A) Whether logic is a science or an instrument.
B) That knowledge and the known are identical in essence.
C) Whether tasawwur is possible without language.
D) That assent is more valuable than conception.

Key Takeaway
The doubt about the identity of knowledge and the known challenges the distinctness of conception and assent, and its solution is a key topic in Islamic epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author leaves the solution hanging. Based on what you know of Islamic logic, propose a possible solution: Is the doubt based on confusing mental existence with external existence? Write 2-3 sentences.
?
Imagine you are a logician responding to the doubt: how would you defend the distinction between tasawwur and tasdiq while acknowledging that knowledge and the known are not completely separate? Use an example from Fiqh or Kalam.

The Necessity of Logic: From the Known to the Unknown
Author argues for the necessity of logic as a safeguard against error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that logic is necessary to prevent error in reasoning and apply it to an Islamic legal example.

Core Idea
The unknown is only partially unknown; logic guards against error in linking known and unknown.

This passage from Bahr al-'Ulum addresses a fundamental question: if something is unknown, how can we seek it? The author shows that the unknown is never absolutely unknown; it is known in some respect. This insight underpins the necessity of a 'protecting law' (qānūn ʿāṣim) – logic – to prevent error when we move from the known to the unknown. In this lesson, you will unpack this argument and see how it applies to Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its resolution
The author resolves an implicit objection: how can we seek the unknown? The resolution is that the unknown aspect is not completely unknown, as it is known in some other respect.
2
Extract the two key premises
Premise 1: The sought thing is known by some of its considerations. Premise 2: Not every ordering (tartīb) is beneficial or natural; opinions conflict. Therefore, a law is needed to protect from error.
3
Connect to the role of logic
The law that protects from error is logic (al-manṭiq). This establishes logic as a normative tool for correct reasoning.
4
Apply to an Islamic legal example
Consider qiyas: the original case (asl) is fully known, the new case (far') is unknown in its ruling but known in the 'illa. Logic ensures the analogy is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحَلُهُ: أَنَّ الْوَجْهَ الْمَجْهُولَ لَيْسَ مَجْهُولًا مُطْلَقًا، حَتَّى يَمْتَنِعَ الطَّلَبُ؛ فَإِنَّ الْوَجْهَ الْمَعْلُومَ وَجْهُهُ، أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّ الْمَطْلُوبَ الْحَقِيقَةُ الْمَعْلُومَةُ بِبَعْضِ اعْتِبَارَاتِهَا، وَلَيْسَ كُلُّ تَرْتِيبٍ مُفِيدًا وَلَا طَبَعِيًّا، وَمِنْ ثَمَّ تَرَى الْآرَاءَ مُتَنَافِضَةً، فَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ قَانُونِ عَاصِمٍ عَنْ الْخَطَا فِيهِ، وَهُوَ الْمَنْطِقُ.
And its resolution: that the unknown aspect is not absolutely unknown, so that seeking is impossible; for the known aspect is its aspect. Do you not see that the sought [thing] is the reality known by some of its considerations? And not every ordering is beneficial nor natural, and therefore you see opinions conflicting. So there must be a law that protects from error in it, and that is logic.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents the justification for the science of logic. The author addresses a potential objection: how can we seek knowledge of something that is unknown? He resolves it by distinguishing between absolute unknown and relative unknown. The aspect sought is not unknown in every way; it is known in some respect (e.g., its genus or property), otherwise the search would be impossible. He then shifts to the practical need for logic: not every method of reasoning leads to truth; opinions conflict because people use defective reasoning. Therefore, a law (qānūn) is necessary to protect the intellect from error, and that law is logic. This establishes logic as a normative tool for correct thinking, similar to how grammar protects speech from error.

Key Terms
الْوَجْهُ الْمَجْهُولُ
قَانُونٍ عَاصِمٍ
الْمَنْطِقُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the unknown aspect can be sought?
A) Because it is absolutely unknown and thus requires divine revelation.
B) Because it is known in some respect, otherwise the search would be impossible.
C) Because every unknown is actually known by intuition.
D) Because the unknown is a figment of imagination.

Key Takeaway
Logic is necessary because the unknown is never absolutely unknown, and without a protective law, reasoning leads to conflicting opinions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a jurist using qiyas to extend the prohibition of ribā to a new financial transaction. Explain how the transaction is 'known in some respect' and why logic (qānūn ʿāṣim) is needed to ensure the analogy is valid.
?
The author says 'not every ordering is beneficial nor natural.' Give an example from Islamic scholarship where a faulty ordering led to a wrong conclusion (e.g., in fiqh or kalām).

Reading a Classical Islamic Logic Title Page
Title page identifying the commentary, author, commentator, and editor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author, commentator, and editor of a classical Islamic logical work from its title page.

Core Idea
A title page is a scholarly pedigree: it tells you who wrote what, and who preserved it.

Before diving into the content of a classical Islamic text, it is essential to understand its scholarly pedigree. A title page (or its manuscript equivalent) typically names the matn (original text), its author, the commentator, and often the editor of the edition you hold. This lesson teaches you to parse such a title page, using the example of 'Sharh Sullam al-'Ulum'.

Guided Path
1
Locate the original text (matn)
Find the title of the base text being commented upon. It often appears with the word 'ala (upon) or after 'Sharh'. In this case, it is 'Sullam al-'Ulum'.
2
Identify the commentator (sharih)
The name of the commentator appears directly after the word 'Sharh' or in the line after it. Here it is 'Abd al-'Ali ibn Nizam al-Din al-Kahnawi.
The commentator's nisbahs (e.g., al-Kahnawi, al-Madrasi) indicate his geographic and scholarly affiliations.
3
Identify the author of the matn
Look for phrases like 'li-' (by) or 'li-imam...' indicating authorship. Here, the author is 'Muhibb Allah ibn 'Abd al-Shakur al-Bihari'.
His other famous work 'Musallam al-Thubut' is mentioned to establish his scholarly authority.
4
Extract the editor's information
The editor (muhaqqiq) is often listed after 'dirasa wa tahqiq' (study and editing). Here it is 'Abd al-Nasir Ahmad al-Shafi'i al-Malibari'.
His credentials (M.A. from Al-Azhar) add to the edition's reliability.
5
Note dates and affiliations
Dates (AH/CE) and madhhab affiliations (e.g., Hanafi, Maturidi) help contextualize the work historically and doctrinally.
The commentator died 1225 AH, the author died 1119 AH.

Source Text

شرح سلم العلوم
مزي

Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a title page from a printed edition of the commentary (Sharh) on the logical text 'Sullam al-'Ulum'. It identifies the key figures involved. The commentary itself is by 'Abd al-'Ali ibn Nizam al-Din al-Kahnawi, a Hanafi-Maturidi scholar from the Indian subcontinent (1142-1225 AH). The original text being commented upon is 'Sullam al-'Ulum' in the science of logic (Mantiq), authored by the Imam Muhibb Allah (written here as 'Yuhibbu Allah') ibn 'Abd al-Shakur al-Bihari, who also wrote the well-known work 'Musallam al-Thubut' in Usul al-Fiqh. This edition has been studied and edited by 'Abd al-Nasir Ahmad al-Shafi'i al-Malibari, who holds a Master's degree from Al-Azhar University. The title page serves to introduce the scholarly pedigree of the work, which is part of the tradition of teaching logic in the Muslim world, particularly in the Indian and Azhari contexts.

Key Terms
سلم العلوم
شرح
علم المنطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The title page provides the essential scholarly lineage of a text: author, commentator, and editor, which is the first step in critically engaging with any classical Islamic work.

Identifying the Scholars Behind Bahr Ulum
Title page identifying the commentary, authors, and editor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three key scholars (author, commentator, editor) involved in the production of Bahr Ulum and explain their contributions to the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
The transmission of Islamic logic relies on a chain of scholars: author, commentator, editor.

This title page introduces the commentary Bahr Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum, a foundational text in Islamic logic. Understanding the roles of the original author, the commentator, and the editor helps us appreciate the scholarly tradition. Let's break down what this page tells us.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original text (matn)
Find the name of the work being commented on. The title page says 'شرح سلم العلوم' – the commentary is on 'سلم العلوم'.
Sullam al-Ulum is a well-known introductory text in logic.
2
Identify the commentator
Who wrote the commentary? The page gives his full name: 'أبو العياش، عبد العلي بن نظام الدين الكهنوي' – he is the commentator.
His nisbah 'al-Kahnawi', 'al-Madrasi', 'al-Hanafi', 'al-Maturidi' indicate his origin and affiliation.
3
Identify the original author
Who wrote Sullam al-Ulum? The page says: 'للإمام الشيخ يُحب الله بن عبد الشكور لعثماني الجنفي البيهاري الهندي' – that is 'Ubayd Allah al-Bihari.
He also authored 'Musallam al-Thubut' in usul al-fiqh.
4
Identify the editor
Who edited this edition? The page mentions: 'دراسة وتحقيق عبد التنصير احمد الشافعيّ المليباري' – the editor is 'Abd al-Tansir Ahmad al-Shafi'i al-Malibari.
His academic credentials include a Master's in Creed and Philosophy from al-Azhar.
5
Map the chain of transmission
Connect the three: al-Bihari (author) -> al-Kahnawi (commentator) -> al-Malibari (editor). This shows the ongoing development of the text.
Each added layer (sharh, tahqiq) enriches the original work.

The Prophet as the Final Cause of Creation
Author praises Prophet, cites hadith and poetry as evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's main claim about the Prophet's status in creation and the evidence he uses to support it.

Core Idea
The author presents the Prophet as the final cause of creation, supported by hadith and poetry.

This preface sets the tone for the entire book. The author immediately places the Prophet at the center of creation, using both prophetic tradition and poetic praise to establish his unmatched status. Understanding this opening helps you recognize the theological framework of the work.

Guided Path
1
Locate the central claim about the Prophet
Find the sentence where the author says the Prophet is like the 'final cause' of creation. What does he mean by this? How does he connect it to the existence of the heavens and earth?
The term 'علة غائية' comes from Aristotelian causality, but is used metaphorically here.
2
Examine the hadith evidence
Read the hadith from Tirmidhi where the Prophet is asked about when prophethood was established. Note the different wording about Adam (between water/clay vs. spirit/body). How does this hadith support the claim?
The author may have adapted the wording for emphasis.
3
Analyze the poetic support
Look at the verse from al-Burda. How does it reinforce the idea that the world exists for the Prophet's sake? What do the commentators (al-Mahalli, al-Anisari) say about it?
The verse states 'without him the world would not have emerged from non-existence'.
4
Understand the author's defensive stance
The author criticizes those who are disturbed by hearing the Prophet's virtues. What does he imply about their methodology and their connection to Islam?
He contrasts their discomfort with their comfort in praising others who taught them a deviant path.

Source Text

مُقَدِّمَةُ الْكِتَابِ
الحمد لله الذي بعث نبينا ببراهين قاطعة، وحجج ساطعة زاهرة، وآيات باهرة، سيد المرسلين، إمام الأولين والآخرين. ذلك الرسول الهاشمي الذي كان نبيا وآدم بين الماء والطين، ولولاه لما خلق السماوات والأرضين، فهو كالعلة الغائية للتكوين. يشهد لما قاله ما رواه أهل الحديث في كتبهم، مثل الإمام الترمذي في المناقب (٥٨٥/٥، رقم الحديث ٣٦٠٩)، بسنده عن سيدنا أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه: قالوا: يا رسول الله، متى وجبت لك النبوة؟ قال: وآدم بين الروح والجسد. قال أبو عيسى: هذا حديث حسن صحيح غريب من حديث أبي هريرة. وهناك من تتضايق صدورهم حين يسمعون فضائل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ومناقبه، ولا يشعرون بأدنى حرج حين يبالغون في ثناء من علمهم هذا المنهج المنحرف، وليت شعري هل لهم من الإسلام نصيب إلا الدعوى؟ قال إمام المحبين في البردة (رقم البيت: ٣٣): وَكَيْفَ تَدْعُو إِلَى الدُّنْيَا ضَرُورَةُ مَنْ لَوْلَاهُ لَمْ تُخْرَجِ الدُّنْيَا مِنَ الْعَدَمِ؟ وقد تلقاه فحول أهل العلم من الأمة بالقبول، من غير أي نكير، منهم الشارح المحقق جلال الدين المحلي، وشيخ الإسلام زكريا الأنصاري... وانظر مثلا ما قاله الإمام الفقيه الشافعي الكبير ابن العماد الأقفهسي رحمه الله (ت: ٨٠٨هـ) في شرحه على هذه البردة (١٩٠) حين شرح هذا البيت: والدنيا بما فيها ما خلقت إلا لأجله. فمن فارق الجماعة فاقد الحجة، ومطموس البصر والبصيرة. المصطفى
Introduction of the Book: Praise be to Allah who sent our Prophet with decisive proofs, brilliant and luminous arguments, and dazzling verses, the master of the messengers, the imam of the first and the last. That Hashimi messenger who was a prophet while Adam was between water and clay, and were it not for him, the heavens and the earths would not have been created, so he is like the final cause of creation. What supports what he said is what the people of hadith narrated in their books, like Imam al-Tirmidhi in al-Manaqib (5/585, hadith number 3609), by his chain from our master Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him: They said: O Messenger of Allah, when was prophethood established for you? He said: While Adam was between spirit and body. Abu Isa said: This is a hasan sahih gharib hadith from Abu Hurayra. And there are those whose chests are distressed when they hear the virtues and merits of the Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him, and they do not feel the least discomfort when they exaggerate in praising those who taught them this deviant methodology. Would that I knew, do they have any share of Islam except the claim? The imam of lovers said in al-Burda (verse 33): 'And how does necessity call you to the world, when without him the world would not have emerged from non-existence?' And the great scholars of the nation received it with acceptance, without any denial, among them the verifying commentator Jalal al-Din al-Mahalli, and Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari... And look, for example, at what the great Shafi'i jurist Ibn al-Imad al-Aqfahsi, may Allah have mercy on him (d. 808 AH), said in his commentary on this Burda (p. 190) when he explained this verse: 'And the world with everything in it was created only for his sake.' So whoever separates from the congregation is devoid of proof, and his sight and insight are extinguished. Al-Mustafa.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is the preface of the book. The author begins with praise of Allah for sending the Prophet with decisive proofs and brilliant arguments. He describes the Prophet as the Hashimi messenger who was a prophet even before Adam was created, stating that without him the heavens and earth would not have been created, likening him to the final cause of creation. To support this, he cites a hadith from Tirmidhi where the Prophet was asked when prophethood was established for him, and he replied that it was while Adam was between spirit and body. The author then criticizes those who are disturbed by hearing the Prophet's virtues but are comfortable with excessive praise of others who taught them a deviant path. He quotes a verse from al-Burda that states without the Prophet the world would not have emerged from non-existence, noting that great scholars like Jalal al-Din al-Mahalli and Zakariyya al-Ansari accepted this. He further cites a commentary by Ibn al-Imad al-Aqfahsi that the world was created only for the Prophet's sake. He concludes that whoever separates from the congregation (i.e., the mainstream Muslim community) lacks proof and is blind.

Key Terms
العلة الغائية (al-‘illah al-ghā’iyyah): The final cause or purpose; the author uses it to indicate that the Prophet is the ultimate purpose of creation. [Note: In falsafah, 'illa ghā'iyya is a technical term from Aristotelian causality; here it is applied theologically to the Prophet.]
براهين قاطعة (barāhīn qāṭi‘ah): Decisive proofs; referring to the clear evidences of the Prophet's mission. [Note: In mantiq, burhān is a technical term for demonstrative proof; here used generally.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author compare the Prophet to in the preface?
A) The efficient cause of the universe
B) The final cause of creation
C) The material cause of the world
D) The formal cause of existence

Key Takeaway
The preface establishes the Prophet's central role in creation using both textual and poetic evidence, setting the stage for the theological discussions to follow.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the logical structure of the author's argument: does he rely on hadith as authoritative, or poetic metaphor, or both? Write a short analysis.
?
Find another example in Islamic literature where a poet (like al-Busiri) or a scholar uses similar language about the Prophet's role in creation.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Prophet as the Final Cause of Creation
Author opens the book with praise of Allah and the Prophet, citing hadith and poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the claim that the Prophet is the final cause of creation and explain the evidence the author provides from hadith and scholarly consensus.

Core Idea
The Prophet is the purpose for which the universe was created.

The author opens his commentary on logic by praising Allah and the Prophet, establishing a theological foundation. He asserts that the Prophet is the final cause (al-illah al-gha'iyyah) of creation, meaning all existence was brought about for his sake. This preface is not mere flattery; it sets the teleological framework for understanding why logic and knowledge matter: to affirm the truths brought by the Prophet. In this lesson, we will examine the proof the author offers for this claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Find the sentence where the author says the Prophet is like the final cause of formation (كَالْعِلَّةِ الْغَائِيَّةِ لِلتَّكْوِينِ). This is the central theological statement.
Final cause = purpose or end for which something exists.
2
Find the supporting hadith
Locate the hadith from al-Tirmidhi: the Prophet said he was a prophet while Adam was between spirit and body. This indicates his prophethood preceded creation, supporting his centrality.
The hadith is used to show pre-existence and purpose.
3
Analyze the poetic evidence
Read the verse from al-Burdah: 'Were it not for him, this world would not have come out of non-existence.' The author cites this as widely accepted by scholars, including al-Mahalli and al-Aqfahsi.
The poem reinforces the claim with poetic authority.
4
Understand the author's rhetorical purpose
Notice how the author criticizes those who are uncomfortable with praising the Prophet but tolerate excessive praise of others. This sets up a defensive posture for the entire commentary.
The preface establishes the right theological stance before delving into logic.

Introduction of the book. Praise be to Allah who sent our Prophet with decisive proofs, shining and bright arguments, and splendid signs, the Master of the Messengers, the Imam of the first and the last. That Hashimi messenger who was a prophet while Adam was between spirit and body; were it not for him, the heavens and earths would not have been created. So he is like the final cause of formation. Indeed, water and clay are some of his virtues (1) which testify to what was narrated by the people of hadith in their books, such as Imam al-Tirmidhi in al-Manaqib (5/585, hadith number 3609), on the authority of our master Abu Hurayrah, may Allah be pleased with him: 'They said: O Messenger of Allah, when was prophethood established for you? He said: While Adam was between spirit and body.' Abu Isa said: This is a good, authentic, and rare hadith from Abu Hurayrah. There are those whose hearts are distressed when they hear the virtues and merits of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and they feel no discomfort when they exaggerate in praising those who taught them this deviant methodology. Would that I knew, do they have any share of Islam other than the claim? (2) The Imam of the lovers said in al-Burdah (verse number 33): 'And how could the necessity of this world call to one who, were it not for him, this world would not have come out of non-existence?' The great scholars of the Ummah have received it with acceptance, without any denial, among them the investigator commentator Jalal al-Din al-Mahalli, and Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari... And consider, for example, what the great Shafi'i jurist Ibn al-'Imad al-Aqfahsi (d. 808 AH) said in his commentary on this Burdah (190) when he explained this verse: 'The world and what it contains were only created for his sake.' So whoever leaves the community has lost the proof, and his sight and insight are blinded. Al-Mustafa. The commentary of Bahr al-'Ulum on Sullam al-'Ulum. His verses are the greatest signs of his Lord, and the manifestation of His beautiful names, Muhammad al-Mustafa, the seal of the message, Ahmad al-Mujtaba, the possessor of intercession. Muhammad is the master of the two worlds and the two weighty beings, and of the two groups, Arabs and non-Arabs. The pinnacle of knowledge concerning him is that he is human and that he is the best of all of Allah's creation. I have not praised Muhammad by my statement, but I have praised my statement by Muhammad. O Allah, send blessings upon him, eternal blessings, by the number of every atom, one hundred thousand thousand times, and likewise upon his family and all his companions, and upon his pure progeny, and upon all the friends of Allah who follow them.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the book, a preface in which the author praises Allah and the Prophet Muhammad. The author begins by thanking Allah for sending the Prophet with decisive proofs and clear signs. He emphasizes the high station of the Prophet, citing a hadith that the Prophet was a prophet while Adam was still between spirit and body, indicating his prophethood existed before creation. This supports the idea that the Prophet is the final cause of creation. The author then criticizes those who are uncomfortable with praising the Prophet while exaggerating in praise of others, implying they lack true Islam. He quotes a verse from the Burdah poem, which states that the world was created for the Prophet's sake, a statement widely accepted by scholars. He also quotes a commentary on that verse by Ibn al-'Imad al-Aqfahsi. The passage ends with more praise of the Prophet in verse and sending blessings upon him. This preface serves to establish the author's theological stance and reverence for the Prophet before commencing the logical commentary.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Defining Iman as Certain Assent: Genus, Differentia, and Religious Criterion
Author defines iman as certain assent and explains religious basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of iman and distinguish the logical definition from the religious criterion.

Core Idea
Iman is firm certain assent; genus is tasdiq, differentia is yaqin jazim, religious basis is the Prophet's saying.

The author of Bahr Ulum opens his commentary by defining the core term 'iman' (faith). He not only gives a logical definition—tasdiq jazim mutabiq—but also specifies the religiously valid form: acceptance of the Prophet's message. This lesson unpacks his definition using the tools of hadd (definition) and shows how it connects to a famous hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the Arabic. The author says: 'الإيمان اليقين الجازم المطابق'. So iman is defined as al-yaqin al-jazim al-mutabiq.
The definiendum is al-iman.
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The genus is tasdiq (assent). The author explicitly relates iman to tasdiq, placing it as a type of affirmation.
Tasdiq is the mental act of affirming correspondence.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia is 'al-yaqin al-jazim al-mutabiq alla dhi la yahtamil al-naqid' — firm, certain, corresponding assent that does not admit its opposite. This distinguishes iman from other forms of tasdiq.
Differentia provides the specific characteristic.
4
Identify the religious criterion
Although iman can be attained in various ways, the religiously valid form is that which comes 'bi-qawl al-rasul' (by the saying of the Messenger). This adds a revealed condition to the logical definition.
The author limits the definition for legal purposes.
5
Test the definition with a hadith
The author cites the hadith: 'Whoever says La ilaha illallah enters Paradise' to show that the utterance of shahada, based on tasdiq, is the religiously valid form. This illustrates the differentia in practice.
The hadith exemplifies the religious criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب
(الْإِيمَانُ بِهِ) ـ تعالى وتقدس ـ بأنه كائن كما هو بأسمائه وصفاته (نعم). من أفعال المدح، وقوله: (التَّصْدِيقُ) فاعله والمخصوص مقدم. والإيمان اليقين الجازم المطابق بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض، وهو يحصل بأنحاء شتى، لكن المعتبر شرعا ما يكون بقول الرسول ، وعليه محمل قوله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم: «من قال لا إله إلا الله دخل الجنة»(۲)، والتفصيل في «الفتوحات المكية للشيخ الأكبر، خاتم الولاية المحمدية، خليفة الله تعالى في الأرضين. ( وَالاِعْتِصَامُ بِهِ حَبَّذَا التَّوْفِيقُ)، ومخصوصه أيضا مقدم.
لما فرغ من الحمد الله الله أراد أن يشرع تبركا في الصلاة على من هو وسيلة في وصول الفيض منه تعالى، وقدوة في الدنيا والآخرة، ورحمة للعالمين كافة ، فقال : (وَالصَّلاةُ وَالسَّلاَمُ عَلَى مَنْ بُعِثَ) رسولا ورحمة ، وهاديا
Introduction of the book: Faith in Him — exalted and sanctified — that He is as He is with His names and attributes (yes). It is of the acts of praise, and His saying: 'al-tasdiq' (assent) — its agent and the specific object are advanced. And faith (iman) is firm certain assenting correspondence such that it does not admit of its opposite, and it is attained in various ways, but what is considered religiously is that which is by the saying of the Messenger, and upon it is carried his saying, peace be upon him and his family: 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah enters Paradise.' The details are in al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya of the Greatest Shaykh, the Seal of Muhammadan Sainthood, the Vicegerent of Allah Almighty in the earths. And 'al-i'tisam bihi habbadha al-tawfiq', and its specific object is also advanced. When he finished praising Allah, he intended to begin with blessings upon the one who is the means of the arrival of emanation from Him, and the exemplar in this world and the Hereafter, and mercy to all the worlds, so he said: 'And prayers and peace be upon the one who was sent' as a messenger, mercy, and a guide.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is part of the introduction to the book 'Bahr Ulum', a commentary on 'Sullam al-'Ulum'. The author explains the meaning of 'iman' (faith) and 'tasdiq' (assent). He begins by stating that faith in Allah is to affirm that He exists as He is with His names and attributes. This is considered an act of praise. Then he analyzes the word 'al-tasdiq' (assent), noting that its agent (the one who assents) and the specific object (the thing assented to) are fronted in the Arabic sentence structure. He then defines iman as firm, certain assent that corresponds to reality such that it does not admit of its opposite. This is a comprehensive definition covering various levels of certainty. However, the legally valid faith (shar'an) is that which is based on the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him), as exemplified by the hadith: 'Whoever says 'La ilaha illallah' enters Paradise.' For further detail, he refers to Ibn Arabi's 'al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya'. After finishing the praise of Allah, the author then begins the blessings upon the Prophet, calling him a means of emanation, a guide, and a mercy. This is a standard format in Islamic texts: after the hamdalah, the author sends salawat upon the Prophet before proceeding to the subject matter.

الإيمان
التصديق
اليقين الجازم

Key Takeaway
The definition of iman as firm certain assent uses the genus of tasdiq and the differentia of yaqin jazim, but its religious validity depends on the Prophet's teaching.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Defining Certainty: The Logical Definition of Iman
Author defines iman as certain knowledge and distinguishes between epistemic and legal aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four components of the logical definition of iman (yaqin, jazim, mutabiq, la yahtamil al-naqid) and apply this schema to evaluate any definition of certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Faith is defined as decisive, conforming certainty that admits no opposite.

In the opening of his commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, the author provides a precise definition of iman that is fundamentally epistemological. This definition is not merely theological but serves as a model for how certain knowledge is characterized in the Islamic logical tradition. Understanding its components will sharpen your ability to distinguish genuine certainty from mere belief or conjecture.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Find the Arabic phrase: 'الإيمان اليقين الجازم المطابق بحيث لا يحتمل النقيض'. This is the full definition.
2
Identify the four components
Break down: 1) yaqin (certainty) – the genus. 2) jazim (decisive) – excludes hesitation. 3) mutabiq (conforming) – must correspond to reality. 4) la yahtamil al-naqid (admits no opposite) – excludes any possibility of falsehood.
This is similar to a 'hadd' (definition) through genus and differentiae.
3
Compare with other definitions
Notice that the author adds a legal condition: 'al-mu'tabar shar'an ma yakun bi-qawl al-rasul'. This means that for legal purposes, faith is accepted only when based on the Prophet's statement, even if epistemic certainty could be attained otherwise.
This shows the interplay between logical certainty and legal requirement.
4
Apply the schema
Now test another supposed certain claim: Is the statement 'Allah exists' certain by this definition? Check if it is decisive, conforming, and admits no opposite.
This exercise reinforces the components.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة الكتاب
والشيخ المقتول (١).
(الْإِيمَانُ بِهِ) ـ تعالى وتقدس ـ بأنه كائن كما هو بأسمائه وصفاته (نعم).

Introduction of the book. And the murdered shaykh (1). Faith in Him—exalted and sanctified—that He is as He is with His names and attributes (yes). From the acts of praise, and His saying: 'The assent'—its agent and the specified are advanced. And faith is decisive, conforming certainty such that it admits no opposite, and it is attained in various ways, but what is legally considered is that which is by the saying of the Messenger, and upon that is based his saying, peace be upon him and his family: 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah enters Paradise' (2). The detail is in 'al-Futuhat al-Makkiyya of the Greatest Shaykh, Seal of Muhammadan Sainthood, Viceroy of Allah Most High in the lands. And holding fast to Him—how excellent is success—and its specified is also advanced. When he finished praising Allah Allah, he intended to begin, seeking blessing, with prayer upon the one who is a means for the arrival of effusion from Him, a model in this world and the Hereafter, and a mercy to all worlds. So he said: 'And prayer and peace be upon the one who was sent as a messenger and mercy and a guide =6 He was at the beginning a Mu'tazili, then he left the cloak of Mu'tazilism to the fold of the Sunna, after a famous debate in which he confounded his master Abu Ali al-Jubba'i in 912 CE, and his method is distinguished by moderation and balance. He has: al-Ibana, Maqalat al-Islamiyyin, and al-Luma', and it is later than al-Ibana. And Imam Ibn Furak has a compilation collecting the Imam's sayings, which the Hafiz Ibn Asakir singled out with a huge work in his defense, namely 'Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari'. See for example Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra by Taj al-Subki: 3/347-444, History of Arabic Literature by Brockelmann: 2/429-433. (1) They are those who say the essence of existence, and as for the doctrine of the majority of theologians who say existence is additional, then quiddities in themselves are quiddities, and effecting and making are in consideration of their being existent, and what follows existence, and the meaning of effecting is making something something, so being described by existence is real. Refer to Abd al-Hakim on Sharh al-Mawaqif: 3/40. (2) This is an authentic hadith, narrated from our master Mu'adh ibn Jabal, may Allah be pleased with him, and reported by Imam Ahmad, may Allah be pleased with him, and al-Daraqutni, and others. Taj al-Subki surveyed multiple chains for it in his Tabaqat al-Kubra, see for example: 1/50, 051. 179 Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum For all creatures to the path of truth, (by the proof) of his message, (which contains healing for every sick one), from heart diseases, such as miserliness, pride, arrogance, compound ignorance, and turning away from obedience to Allah Most High and Glorious. And that proof is the noble Qur'an, which if someone acts by it, he reaches the pinnacle of perfection, and the secrets of Allah Most High are unveiled. It has an outward and an inward. Man becomes acquainted with its outward by serving the books of hadith and Arabic, but he does not become acquainted with its inward except by arduous exercises and complete struggles. And upon what its outward informs, there is nothing wet or dry but it is in a clear, hidden book. And if you wish to know the Qur'anic subtleties, look at the books of the Shaykh with the eye of fairness, and your heart free from injustice and bias. 6 · (And upon his family), meaning his noble progeny, (and his companions), meaning those who attained the companionship of the Prophet, peace be upon him and his family, with faith, and it was long (1), (who are the introductions of the religion), meaning those upon whose love the religion depends; for hatred of them the weight of a mustard seed necessitates hatred of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family, which removes from faith. The Prophet, peace be upon him and his family, said: 'Allah, Allah, regarding my companions: whoever loves them loves them by My love, and whoever hates them hates them by My hatred, and whoever harms them harms Me, and whoever harms Me harms Allah, and Allah is about to seize him' (2), narrated by al-Tirmidhi. (And the proofs of guidance), meaning those that indicate what leads to the desired goal (3). (And certainty), meaning faith in Allah Most High and His Messenger. Allah said: Harmed (1) Thus in the original, and it is not hidden that length of companionship is not a condition for being a companion. (2) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi in his Sunan, Book of Virtues, from our master Abdullah ibn Mughaffal: 5/696, number 3862. (3) According to the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna; as for the Mu'tazila, guidance according to them is the indication to the desired goal, not to what leads to it. Refer to the introduction of the commentary of both al-Dawani and al-Yazdi on al-Tahdhib for more detail. 180
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the commentary 'Bahr al-Ulum' on the logical text 'Sullam al-Ulum'. The author begins with the traditional hamdalah (praise of God) and then defines the key term 'iman' (faith). He states that iman is decisive, conforming certainty that admits no opposite (al-yaqin al-jazim al-mutabiq bi-haythu la yahtamil al-naqid). This is a theological definition commonly used in Kalam and logic to distinguish certain knowledge from mere opinion. He notes that while faith can be attained through various means, legally (shar'an) it is based on the utterance of the Prophet. He then cites the hadith 'Whoever says there is no god but Allah enters Paradise' as support. The author then transitions to blessings on the Prophet, describing him as the means of divine effusion and a mercy to the worlds. Following this, a lengthy editorial footnote discusses the biography of al-Ash'ari (likely the 'murdered shaykh') and his theological position, as well as a hadith authentication. On the next page, the author continues by identifying the proof (al-dalil) as the Qur'an, which heals spiritual diseases. He distinguishes between its apparent meaning (zahr) accessible through study of hadith and Arabic, and its inner meaning (batn) attainable only through spiritual exercises. The passage concludes with praise of the Prophet's family and companions, citing hadith about the necessity of loving them. This entire opening is typical of Islamic scholarly works, combining theological definitions, scriptural citations, and traditional praise, setting a reverent tone for the logical treatise that follows.

Key Terms

التصديق
اليقين

Checkpoint
Which component of the definition of iman ensures that it cannot be false?
A) Yaqin (certainty)
B) Jazim (decisive)
C) Mutabiq (conforming)
D) La yahtamil al-naqid (admits no opposite)

Key Takeaway
Faith in the Islamic logical tradition is defined as decisive certainty that conforms to reality and excludes any possibility of falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Using the four components from the definition, analyze the statement: 'The Qur'an is the word of Allah.' Is this certain by the definition? Check each component: decisive? conforming? admits no opposite? Explain.
?
2. Apply the same schema to another theological claim: 'Muhammad is the Seal of the Prophets.' Identify the genus (prophet), differentia (seal), and check if it fits the definition of certain knowledge.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Logic as the Balance: The Purpose and Title of Sullam al-Ulum
Commentator introduces the treatise on logic, the Ladder of Sciences, with praise and purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logic is called 'the art of the balance' and state the significance of the title 'Sullam al-Ulum'.

Core Idea
Logic is the balance for weighing arguments, and this text is the ladder to climb the sciences.

In this opening passage, the commentator introduces his work on logic, which he calls 'the art of the balance'. He emphasizes that the ultimate proof is the Qur'an, but logic serves as a tool to reason soundly. Understanding this metaphor helps us see why logic is essential for distinguishing truth from falsehood in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the passage
Read the first few lines and find what the commentator says about the Qur'an as the ultimate proof and the purpose of this treatise.
Pay attention to the phrase 'صناعة الميزان'.
2
Locate the naming of the treatise
Find the exact words where the commentator says: 'هذه رسالة في صناعة الميزان سميتها بسلم العلوم'. Note the name and the description.
The treatise is named Sullam al-Ulum.
3
Understand the metaphors
Why is logic called a 'balance' (ميزان)? What does 'ladder of sciences' (سلم العلوم) imply about the role of logic?
Think about how a balance weighs things and a ladder helps ascend.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
How will this treatise on logic help in studying other sciences like tafsir, fiqh, and kalam?
Recall that logic provides tools for sound reasoning.
