شرح بحر العلوم على سلم العلوم
للخلائق إلى سبيل الحق، (بالدليل) على رسالته، (الَّذِي فِيهِ شِفَاءٌ لِكُلِّ عَلِيل)، بالعلل القلبية، كالبخل والكبر والنخوة والجهل المركب، والإعراض عن طاعة الله تعالى وسبحانه . وذلك الدليل هو القرآن الشريف، الذي إذا عمل به أحد وصل ذروة الكمال، وانكشف أسرار الله تعالى، وله ظهر وبطن، والإنسان يطلع على ظهره بخدمته كتب الأحاديث والعربية ولا يطلع على بطنه إلا برياضات شاقة ، ومجاهدات تامة، وعلى ما أخبر بذلك ظهره ولا رطب ولا يابس إلا هو في كتاب مبين مكنون فيه. وإن شئت أن تعلم النكات القرآنية فانظر كتب الشيخ بعين الإنصاف، وقلبك خال عن الجور والاعتساف .
وَعَلَى آلِهِ ، أي عترته الأخيار، (وَأَصْحَابِهِ)، أي الذين أدركوا صحبة النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم مع الإيمان، وطالت، (الَّذِينَ هُمْ مُقَدِّمَاتُ الدِّينِ)، أي الذين يتوقف الدين على محبتهم؛ فإن بغضهم مثقال ذرة مستلزم لبغض رسول الله صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ ، المبعد عن الإيمان قال النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم: «الله الله في أصحابي من أحبهم فبحبي أحبهم، ومن أبغضهم فببغضي أبغضهم ، ومن آذاهم فقد آذاني، ومن آذاني فقد آذى الله، ومن الله فيوشك أن يأخذه» . (وَحُجَجُ الْهِدَايَةِ)، أي الدالة على ما يوصل إلى المطلوب، (وَالْيَقِين)، أي الإيمان بالله تعالى ورسوله، قال الله تعالى: ليغيظَ بِهِمُ الْكُفَّارَ [الفتح / ٢٩] .
(أَمَّا بَعْد حمد الله والصلاة على رسوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام، حذف منه المضاف إليه ، فبني على الضم (فَهَذِهِ)، أي الأمر المرتب الحاضر في الذهن بوجه من الألفاظ المخصوصة الدالة على المعاني المخصوصة، أو المعاني المخصوصة المدلول عليها بالألفاظ المخصوصة ( رِسَالَةٌ) في صِنَاعَةِ الْمِيزَانِ ، سَمَّيْتُهَا بِـسُلَّمِ الْعُلُومِ»، اللَّهُمَّ اجْعَلْهُ بَيْنَ الْمُتُونِ كَالشَّمْس بَيْنَ النُّجُوم.
The Explanation of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. For creatures to the path of truth, (by proof) upon His message, (which contains healing for every ill), regarding spiritual ailments such as miserliness, arrogance, haughtiness, and compound ignorance, and turning away from obedience to Allah Almighty. And that proof is the noble Qur'an; when one acts by it, he reaches the pinnacle of perfection, and the secrets of Allah Almighty are unveiled. It has an outward and an inward. Man attains its outward by serving the books of hadith and Arabic, but does not attain its inward except through arduous spiritual exercises and complete struggles. And upon what I have informed you, its outward is that there is nothing wet or dry except it is in a clear book contained therein. And if you wish to learn the subtle points of the Qur'an, look at the books of the Shaykh with an eye of fairness, and your heart free from injustice and oppression. And upon his family, i.e., his chosen progeny, and his companions, i.e., those who attained the companionship of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, with faith, and it was prolonged, who are the introductions of the religion, i.e., the religion depends on loving them; for hating them to the weight of a speck necessitates hating the Messenger of Allah, Allah bless him and grant him peace, which distances from faith. The Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, said: 'Fear Allah concerning my companions! Whoever loves them loves them out of love for me, and whoever hates them hates them out of hatred for me. Whoever harms them has harmed me, and whoever harms me has harmed Allah, and from Allah He may soon seize him.' And the proofs of guidance, i.e., indicating what leads to the desired end, and certainty, i.e., faith in Allah Almighty and His Messenger. Allah Almighty said: 'That He may enrage the disbelievers with them.' [Al-Fath 48:29]. To proceed: after praise of Allah and prayers upon His Messenger, his family, and his companions, prayers and peace. The genitive construct is omitted, so it is built on damma. 'This' i.e., the ordered matter present in the mind by some specific words indicating specific meanings, or the specific meanings indicated by specific words, is a treatise on the art of the balance, which I named 'Sullam al-Ulum', O Allah, make it among the texts like the sun among the stars.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the commentary (sharh) on the famous logic text 'Sullam al-Ulum' (The Ladder of the Sciences). The commentator begins by praising Allah and explaining that the true guide to truth is the Qur'an, which he describes as the ultimate proof (al-dalil) for spiritual ailments. He then sends blessings upon the Prophet, his family, and companions, emphasizing their role as foundations of the religion and warning against hatred of them, quoting a hadith. After this, he introduces the treatise itself: 'This is a treatise on the art of the balance (ṣinā‘at al-mīzān), which I named Sullam al-Ulum.' The phrase 'art of the balance' is a classical term for logic (mantiq) because logic weighs arguments to distinguish truth from falsehood. The commentator expresses a hope that his work will shine among other texts like the sun among the stars. The passage establishes the purpose of the book—teaching logic as a tool for sound reasoning within an Islamic framework.

صناعة الميزان
سلم العلوم

Key Takeaway
Logic is the balance for weighing arguments, and this text, Sullam al-Ulum, is a ladder to master the sciences.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Understanding the Author's Preface and the Metaphor of Logic as Balance
Author opens with Quranic verse, praises Allah, and introduces his book title and purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the metaphor of 'al-Mizan' for logic and identify the purpose of the author's title 'Sullam al-Ulum'.

Core Idea
Logic is the Balance of the sciences; Sullam al-Ulum is the ladder to master it.

Every journey into a discipline begins with understanding its purpose. The author of Sullam al-Ulum opens his work with a clear statement: logic is the 'Balance' that weighs arguments. This preface sets the stage for the entire science of logic by using a powerful metaphor. In this lesson, you will unpack the key elements of this opening and see why the author chose the title 'Ladder of the Sciences'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qur'anic verse
Find the verse from Surah al-Fath (48:29) that the author quotes. Note that it is about the Prophet's companions. Why might the author begin with this? Consider the theme of distinguishing truth from falsehood.
The verse sets a tone of righteous distinction.
2
Analyze the author's formula
The phrase 'Amma ba'd' (to proceed) is the standard transition after praise. The author then defines 'fa-hadhihi' (so this). He explains it refers to an arranged subject expressed through specific words and meanings. Recognize that this is a typical Islamic scholarly introduction.
This formula anchors the text in tradition.
3
Understand the metaphor of 'al-Mizan'
The author says his treatise is on 'sina'at al-mizan' (the craft of the Balance). In Islamic logic, al-Mizan is a common metaphor for logic itself: just as a scale weighs physical objects, logic weighs proofs to separate truth from falsehood.
This metaphor appears in many works, e.g., al-Ghazali's 'Mizan al-'Amal'.
4
Interpret the title and supplication
The author names his book 'Sullam al-Ulum' (Ladder of the Sciences). A ladder implies ascent and progress. He ends with a prayer that his book be among texts like the sun among stars, indicating his hope for its prominence and utility.
The title echoes the famous didactic poem 'al-Sullam' by al-Akhdari.

تعالى: ليغيظَ بِهِمُ الْكُفَّارَ [الفتح / ٢٩] . (أَمَّا بَعْد حمد الله والصلاة على رسوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام، حذف منه المضاف إليه ، فبني على الضم (فَهَذِهِ)، أي الأمر المرتب الحاضر في الذهن بوجه من الألفاظ المخصوصة الدالة على المعاني المخصوصة، أو المعاني المخصوصة المدلول عليها بالألفاظ المخصوصة ( رِسَالَةٌ) في صِنَاعَةِ الْمِيزَانِ ، سَمَّيْتُهَا بِـسُلَّمِ الْعُلُومِ»، اللَّهُمَّ اجْعَلْهُ بَيْنَ الْمُتُونِ كَالشَّمْس بَيْنَ النُّجُوم.
He, exalted, said: 'That He may enrage the disbelievers' [al-Fath 29]. (To proceed: after praise of Allah and prayer upon His Messenger, his family and companions—may peace and blessings be upon him—the annexed element being omitted, it is built on damma. (So this), meaning the arranged matter present in the mind by way of specific expressions indicating specific meanings, or specific meanings indicated by specific expressions, (is a treatise) on the craft of the Balance. I have named it 'Sullam al-Ulum'. O Allah, make it among the texts like the sun among the stars.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is the author's preface to his work 'Sullam al-Ulum' (The Ladder of Sciences). He begins with a Quranic verse from Surah al-Fath, then follows the traditional formula 'Amma ba'd' after praising Allah and sending blessings on the Prophet. He explains that the phrase 'fa-hadhihi' (so this) refers to a mentally arranged subject expressed through specific words and meanings. He declares that this is a treatise on the craft of 'al-Mizan' (the Balance), which is logic, and he has named it 'Sullam al-Ulum'. He concludes with a supplication for his book to be among the prominent texts like the sun among the stars, indicating his high aspiration for its acceptance and influence.

Key Terms
الميزان
سلم العلوم

A) The scales of justice on the Day of Judgment
B) The science of logic
C) A physical balance for weighing goods
D) A specific book on arithmetic

Key Takeaway
Logic is called the Balance because it weighs arguments, and Sullam al-Ulum is the ladder to climb that science.

Metaphysical Foundation of the Five Universals
Author introduces the chapter on the five universals with a theological discourse on the perfect man.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the commentator's theological purpose in introducing the five universals with a discussion of al-insan al-kamil.

Core Idea
The five universals are grounded in the reality of the perfect man.

This passage opens the chapter on the five universals, but instead of immediate definitions, the commentator presents a theological cosmology. He argues that the study of universals is inseparable from the concept of al-insan al-kamil — the perfect man who encompasses all divine names. This prelude alerts you that logic in Islam is never a neutral tool; it is embedded in a worldview where knowledge, being, and sainthood converge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pre-creation state
The passage begins by describing God's direct management of the world before man. Each part manifested a specific divine name, and each name sought its own manifestation, causing potential conflict. Only God's preservation kept things in balance. Notice the emphasis on God's sole agency.
2
Understand the creation of the perfect man
God then created man who encompasses all names and realities. This man is not the maker or sustainer but a means through which God's overflow reaches creation. The commentator stresses that man is merely a vessel, not a partner in divinity. This corrects a possible misunderstanding.
3
Identify the role of Muhammad as the ultimate perfect man
The passage identifies Prophet Muhammad as the true vicegerent. Other prophets were his deputies before his physical advent; after his death, the saints (awliya) serve as his deputies. The world's preservation depends on the existence of the perfect man in each age.
4
Connect to the five universals
The five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) are the logical categories used to define and describe reality. The commentator's prelude suggests that these universals are not arbitrary mental constructs but correspond to the structure of existence exemplified in the perfect man. Understanding this helps you see the deeper purpose of logic in Islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكليات الخمس
واعلم : الله سبحانه قبل وجود الإنسان في النشأة العنصرية كان هو بنفسه يتصرف في العالم يُبْقِيهِ، وكل واحد واحد من أجزاء العالم مظهر لاسم اسم ، وكل اسم يطلب ظهور نفسه دون غيره، فكل موجود ضد لآخر يستدعي عدمه، وإنما يبقى كل بإبقاء الله سبحانه، فلما خلق الله الإنسان الجامع لجميع الأسماء وحقائق العالم، وسائر الأشياء بالنسبة إليه، كالقوى، جعله خليفة يتصرف في العالم بإيصال فيضه على حسب استعداد كل وإعطاء العطايا، ويبقى العالم، وليس المراد أن الجاعل والمبقي الإنسان؛ فإنه كفر، بل المراد المبقي والجاعل والمعطي هو الله، والإنسان وسيلة فيه، فجعله الله ختما على خزائن العالم، والخليفة المتصرف حقيقة سيدنا وسيد الأولين والآخرين محمد، مقدم الأنبياء والأولياء، صلوات الله وسلامه عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه وأتباعه أجمعين.
وقبل مجيئه في عالم العناصر كان سائر الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم نوابا له عليه وآله الصلاة والسلام في زمنهم وإذا بعث هو استنار بنوره العالم، وبعد وفاته الأقطاب نواب له ، بهم يحفظ العالم، وإذا انتقل الإنسان الكامل، بأن يموت خاتم الولاية المطلقة ، ينشق السماء ويقوم القيامة، وينقل العمارة إلى الدار الآخرة. فهذه عقائد حقة عليها الأولياء الكرام. اللهم رب ثبت قلبي على الإيمان، وباعدني عن وساوس الأفكار الرذيلة، وأذقني من مکاشفات خاتم الولاية المحمدية شيئا يستقيم به الإيمان، آمین ،آمین، وصل على خير خلقه محمد وآله وأصحابه أجمعين . وإذا بلغ كلامنا هذا النصاب فلنرجع إلى ما كنا فيه من شرح المتن.
Chapter on the Five Universals
And know: God Almighty, before the existence of man in the elemental stage, was Himself managing the world, sustaining it, and every single part of the world is a manifestation of a name, and every name seeks its own manifestation over others, so every existent is opposite to another requiring its non-existence, and each only remains by God's preservation. When God created man who encompasses all names and the realities of the world, and all other things are relative to him like powers, He made him a vicegerent managing the world by conveying His overflow according to each one's preparedness and giving gifts, and the world remains. It is not meant that the maker and sustainer is man, for that is disbelief; rather, the sustainer, maker, and giver is God, and man is a means therein. So God made him a seal upon the treasuries of the world. The vicegerent who truly manages is our master, the master of the first and the last, Muhammad, the foremost of prophets and saints, may God's blessings and peace be upon him and his family, companions, wives, and followers all.
Before his coming in the elemental world, the other prophets, peace be upon them, were his deputies in their time. When he is sent, the world is illuminated by his light. After his death, the poles [of sainthood] are his deputies; through them the world is preserved. When the perfect man departs, i.e., when the seal of absolute sainthood dies, the sky splits, the resurrection occurs, and [the world's] habitation is transferred to the hereafter. These are true beliefs held by the noble saints. O God, O Lord, establish my heart upon faith, distance me from the whispers of vile thoughts, and grant me something of the unveilings of the Muhammadan seal of sainthood by which faith is established. Amen, amen, and bless the best of His creation Muhammad and his family and companions all.
When our speech reaches this point, let us return to what we were in, from the commentary on the text.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a theological prelude to the logical chapter on the five universals (al-kulliyyat al-khams). The commentator begins by explaining the metaphysical necessity of a perfect man (al-insan al-kamil) as a manifestation of all divine names, serving as the vicegerent (khalifa) of God to sustain the world. He emphasizes that God is the true agent, while man is a means. This is tied to the reality of Prophet Muhammad, peace be upon him, as the ultimate perfect man, with earlier prophets as his deputies. The passage then describes the cosmic role of the saints (awliya) after the Prophet, culminating in the eschatological event when the seal of sainthood departs. The commentator concludes with a supplication and a statement returning to the main commentary on the text (sharh al-matan). This shows that the logical discussion of the five universals is framed within a broader metaphysical and spiritual context, reminding the student that the logical tools are rooted in the realities of existence.

Key Terms
الكليات الخمس
الإنسان الكامل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the role of the perfect man in relation to the world?
A) He is the direct creator and sustainer of the world.
B) He is a means through which God sustains the world, not the true agent.
C) He is merely a passive observer of God's actions.
D) He controls the world independently of God.

Key Takeaway
The commentator frames the study of the five universals within a cosmological and spiritual context, linking logic to the reality of the perfect man.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How to Parse a Scholarly Bibliography in the Islamic Logic Tradition
Bibliography listing 24 logic and philosophy references used in the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify key reference works in Islamic logic from a bibliography and trace the commentarial chain linking matn, sharh, and hashiya.

Core Idea
A commentary's bibliography is a map of the intellectual tradition.

This passage from Bahr Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum lists 24 works referenced in the commentary. At first glance, it is a simple list. But for a student of Mantiq, this list reveals the core textbooks, the sequence of commentaries and supercommentaries, and the scholarly networks that produced them. By learning to read this bibliography, you gain the ability to navigate the wider Mantiq literature and understand how later scholars built on earlier ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify key matn (textbooks)
Look for foundational titles like 'Sullam al-Ulum', 'Risala al-Shamsiyya', 'Tajrid al-‘Aqa’id'. These are core texts that were widely taught and commented upon.
2
Identify the sharh (commentary) tradition
Entries like 'Tahrir al-Qawa‘id al-Mantiqiyya' (item 12) are commentaries on a base text. Note the author and which original work is being explained.
3
Spot the hashiya (supercommentaries)
Supercommentaries (hasiyas) take a commentary as their base. Item 22, 'Hashiyat al-Jurjani', is a supercommentary on item 12.
4
Check the author's footnotes
The footnotes (marked 1) alert the reader to printing errors. This teaches critical awareness of edition quality.
Such footnotes are common in scholarly editions.
5
Recognize the scope of sources
The bibliography includes histories (Brockelmann, al-Bayhaqi) and polemical works (Tahafut). This shows the interdisciplinary nature of Islamic logic.

This passage is a bibliography (kashaf al-masadir wa al-maraji') from the commentary Bahr Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. This bibliography serves as a guide to the sources the author relied upon, giving students an overview of the key texts in Islamic logic and related disciplines.

How to Read a Classical Islamic Bibliography
List of references cited by the author in the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a classical Islamic bibliography and explain its role in scholarly works.

Core Idea
A scholarly bibliography is not a list—it is a map of intellectual lineage.

This lesson introduces you to the bibliography (kashf al-masadir) appended to the commentary Bahr al-Ulum. This list is more than a reference; it reveals the author's intellectual debts and the interdisciplinary nature of his work. By learning to read such bibliographies, you will gain the skills to evaluate any Islamic scholarly work's foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the organizing principle
Notice the numerical sequence (52-70). This suggests the bibliography continues from earlier pages. The author likely ordered sources by subject or importance.
The numbering helps cross-reference within the text.
2
Categorize works by subject
Look at titles and authors: Sharh al-Aqa'id (kalam), Sharh al-Mulla Hasan (logic), Sahih al-Bukhari (hadith), Ghayat al-Wusul (usul al-fiqh). Group them to see the disciplinary range.
This reveals the author's scholarly breadth.
3
Note publication details
Each entry includes editor, publisher, city, and date. These details help verify the edition and historical context.
Knowing the edition is crucial for accurate citation.
4
Understand the author's self-reference
Entry 62 lists the author's own work, Fawatih al-Rahmut. This shows his contribution and how he positions himself within the tradition.
Authors often cite their own works to establish authority.
5
Assess the bibliography's completeness
The list includes primary and secondary sources, but note any gaps (e.g., no early logic texts). This indicates the author's selective focus.
A bibliography is always selective; it reflects the author's priorities.

Commentary
This passage is a bibliography (kashf al-masadir wa al-maraji') from the end of the book Bahr Ulum. It lists the primary sources that the author, 'Abd al-'Ali Bahr al-'Ulum, relied upon in his commentary on Sullam al-'Ulum. The list includes works on theology (e.g., Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya), logic (e.g., Sharh al-Mulla Hasan 'ala Sullam al-'Ulum), hadith (Sahih al-Bukhari), jurisprudence (e.g., Sharh Lub al-Usul), philosophy (e.g., 'Uyun al-Hikma by Ibn Sina), and the history of the Islamic sciences (e.g., Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya al-Kubra). The inclusion of these diverse references demonstrates the interdisciplinary nature of the commentary, drawing from the classical Islamic intellectual tradition. The bibliography is arranged numerically from 52 to 70, suggesting that earlier entries may have appeared on preceding pages. The author also lists his own work (Fawatih al-Rahmut) and works by prominent scholars such as al-Farabi and Haji Khalifa. This provides the reader with a scholarly context for the commentary.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
Tracing Intellectual Lineages: Interdisciplinarity in Bahr al-Ulum
List of references cited by the author in the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize the major works cited in Bahr al-Ulum by Islamic discipline and evaluate their relevance to the study of logic.

Core Idea
The bibliography of Bahr al-Ulum shows that logic in Islam was never isolated—it intertwined with theology, philosophy, and jurisprudence.

This lesson builds on the previous one by focusing on the disciplinary diversity of the sources cited. The author of Bahr al-Ulum, a commentary on a logic text (Sullam al-Ulum), did not limit himself to logic works; he drew from kalam, usul al-fiqh, hadith, and philosophy. Understanding this interdisciplinarity is key to appreciating how Islamic scholars approached logic.

Guided Path
1
Extract works related to kalam (theology)
Identify entries on theological topics: Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya (entry 52), Sharh al-Mawaqif (57), Muhassal Afkar (67). These are kalam works that discuss divine attributes and proofs.
Kalam is the main source of arguments for the existence of God.
2
Extract works related to logic (mantiq)
Look for commentaries on logic texts: Sharh al-Mulla Hasan 'ala Sullam al-'Ulum (53, 56), Lawami' al-Asrar (65), and the modern Madkhal (70). These directly engage with logic.
The core logic references indicate the textual tradition.
3
Extract works on usul al-fiqh (legal theory)
Find entries like Sharh Lub al-Usul (55), Ghayat al-Wusul (61), Fawatih al-Rahmut (62), and Mukhtasar al-Muntaha (69). These show the connection between logic and jurisprudence.
Usul scholars used logic to structure legal reasoning.
4
Extract philosophical and historical works
Note works like 'Uyun al-Hikma (60) by Ibn Sina, Kitab al-Huruf (63) by al-Farabi, and Tabaqat al-Shafi'iyya (59). These provide philosophical depth and historical context.
Philosophy and history enrich the logical analysis.
5
Analyze how these disciplines support logic
Consider how a kalam work (e.g., Muhassal) provides ontological foundations, a usul work (e.g., Fawatih al-Rahmut) offers argumentation rules, and a philosophical work (e.g., 'Uyun al-Hikma) supplies conceptual frameworks.
Logic is the common language across these fields.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Parsing the Structure of a Logic Text: Bahr Ulum's TOC
Table of contents of Bahr Ulum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse the table of contents of a classical Islamic logic text and identify its main divisions and key topics.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a blueprint for understanding a text's pedagogical structure and priorities.

The table of contents of Bahr Ulum reveals how the book is organized to introduce the student to the rational sciences, starting with a discussion of their status in Islam and then moving to specific contributions. By learning to parse such a table, you gain a map of the intellectual terrain and can better follow the author's argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main parts
Look at the highest-level divisions: Part One is a study about the book itself, focusing on India and the rational sciences. Part Two is a brief glimpse at the author, Muhibb Allah al-Bahari. This shows that the book contextualizes its subject before presenting it.
2
List chapters under Part One
Part One contains two chapters: Chapter 1 on Islam and the rational sciences, and Chapter 2 on India's share in the rational sciences. Each chapter begins with an introduction.
The order implies a movement from general Islamic framework to specific regional contribution.
3
Examine sections under each chapter
Under Chapter 1, sections cover: (1) the ruling on engaging in rational sciences, (2) their development in Islam, (3) the importance of studying logic. Under Chapter 2, sections cover: beginnings of scientific movement in India, development, intellectual trends, and Arab-Indian dialogue.
Notice the logical progression: from ruling, to history, to importance; then from origins to dialogue.
4
Recognize the editorial and authorial framing
The TOC opens with an editor's opening and ends with Part Two on the author. This frames the main content (Part One) within a scholarly apparatus: editorial context and biographical glimpse.
This is common in academic editions of classical texts.
5
Connect structure to purpose
The TOC reveals the author's pedagogical strategy: first establish the legitimacy and importance of rational sciences (especially logic) in Islam, then demonstrate India's contribution, and finally introduce the author. This structure prepares the reader to appreciate the main logical content that follows.
Understanding this helps you read the book actively, not passively.

فهرس محتويات الكتاب

الموضوع
افتتاحية المحقق
القسم الأول: الدراسة حول الكتاب
الباب الأول: الهند والعلوم العقلية
تمهید
الفصل الأول: الإسلام والعلوم العقلية
المبحث الأول: حكم الاشتغال بالعلوم العقلية
المبحث الثاني: تطور العلوم العقلية في الإسلام
المبحث الثالث: أهمية دراسة المنطق

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the work Bahr Ulum, which is a study of logic (mantiq) and its related disciplines. The table of contents shows that the book is divided into two main parts. The first part is a study about the book itself, focusing on India and the rational sciences. It includes chapters on Islam and the rational sciences, with sections on the ruling of engaging in rational sciences, their development in Islam, and the importance of studying logic. Then it discusses India's contribution, covering the beginnings of scientific activity, development, intellectual trends, and Arab-Indian dialogue. The second part is a brief glimpse at the author, the scholar Muhibb Allah al-Bahari. This table of contents gives the reader an overview of the book's structure and the topics covered.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
5 min
Navigating the Structure of Bahr Ulum
Lists the sections and subsections of Bahr Ulum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main sections of Bahr Ulum and distinguish between the author's biography and the commentator's biography.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a roadmap; in Bahr Ulum, it reveals the book's dual focus on the text and its commentators.

Before diving into the logical content of Bahr Ulum, we must first understand how the book is organized. The table of contents serves as a guide, showing us the major sections and their subheadings. By parsing this structure, we learn what topics are covered and in what order, which helps us later locate specific discussions on logic, the author, and the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main parts
The table lists three major sections: 'القسم الأول: الدراسة حول الكتاب' (First Part: Study about the Book), 'الباب الثاني: لمحة موجزة عن المصنف' (Second Section: A Brief Glimpse of the Author), and 'الباب الثالث: نبذة يسيرة عن الشارح' (Third Section: A Brief Account of the Commentator).
Notice that the second section is about the author (Muhibb Allah al-Bahari) and the third about the commentator (Bahr Ulum).
2
Examine the subsections of the first part
The first part contains chapters on 'Islam and the Intellectual Sciences' and 'India's Share in the Intellectual Sciences'. Each includes subsections on topics like the ruling on studying intellectual sciences, their development, and the importance of logic.
These subsections set the historical and theological context for the logical content of the commentary.
3
Locate the author's biography
Under 'الباب الثاني', find subsections detailing the author's name, upbringing, works, personality, and death. Note that 'المصنف' here refers to the author, not a chapter title.
This section helps us appreciate the background of the original text (Sullam al-Ulum).
4
Locate the commentator's biography
Under 'الباب الثالث', find subsections on the commentator's name, birth, works, and genius. This section also introduces the commentary itself, including its attribution, methodology, and sources.
Understanding the commentator's profile aids in interpreting his commentary style.
5
Recognize the purpose of the table
The table is not just an index; it highlights the book's dual focus: a study of intellectual sciences (including logic) and biographical information on the two scholars. This structure is common in scholarly editions of commentaries.
As you study Bahr Ulum, refer back to this table to orient yourself within the text.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Navigating the Table of Contents of a Logical Commentary
Table of contents of Sharh Bahr al-Ulum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main sections of a classical logical commentary (biography, introduction, logical topics) and locate specific discussions from the table of contents.

Core Idea
The table of contents is a blueprint for the logical journey of the text.

When you open a classical work like 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum', the table of contents is your first guide. It reveals the author's overall structure: biographical background, methodological introduction, and the core logical discussions. In this lesson, you will learn to parse this structure and connect it to the actual content of the commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major divisions
Look for section headings like 'الباب', 'الفصل', 'المبحث'. In this TOC, note the chapters: 'الباب الثالث', 'الفصل الأول', 'الفصل الثاني'.
These divisions help you understand the hierarchy of the text.
2
Locate the biographical section
Find the sections that discuss the author (Bahr al-Ulum). They are listed under 'الفصل الأول شخصية العلامة بحر العلوم'.
Biographical sections are often placed first to introduce the commentator.
3
Locate the introduction and methodology
Find 'الفصل الثاني تعريف بكتاب شرح سلم العلوم' and the preliminary discussions that explain the book's subject and method.
This section sets the stage for the logical content.
4
Find the logical topics
After the introduction, the TOC lists logical topics: 'مقدمة علم المنطق', 'تقسيم العلم', 'الإشكال المترتب', etc.
These topics follow the traditional sequence of a logic textbook.

Using the Table of Contents to Navigate Bahr Ulum
Table of contents of Bahr Ulum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to locate the major sections of Bahr Ulum using its table of contents.

Core Idea
The table of contents is your roadmap to the logical structure of the book.

When you first open a classical commentary like Bahr Ulum, the table of contents is your best friend. It tells you where the author discusses each topic. In this lesson, we will learn how to parse the table of contents to find key sections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main title
Look at the top of the passage: 'فهرس محتويات الكتاب' - this is the table of contents. Then notice the structure: 'القسم الثاني التحقيق' indicates the second section.
2
Find the logic introduction
Scan for 'مقدمة علم المنطق' - that is the introduction to logic. This is a major section that covers the need for logic, definition of knowledge, etc.
The introduction to logic is often the foundation.
3
Locate the division of knowledge
After the introduction, find 'تقسيم العلم إلى تصور وتصديق' - the division of knowledge into concept and assent.
4
Identify the four main issues
Look for 'أمهات المطالب أربعة' and note them: ما (what), أي (which), هل (whether), لم (why).
These correspond to the four questions one asks in any inquiry.

Index of book contents. Subject: Second section: Verification. Text of Sullam al-Ulum. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Wusul. Introduction of the book. Some virtues of the Chosen One, may God bless him and grant him peace. Investigations related to some attributes of transcendence mentioned in the author's speech. People's schools in the attribute of God's knowledge. Investigations related to the act of making. Introduction to the science of logic, including the statement of the need for logic, leading to its definition by description: knowledge is intuitive, not known. Refining the reality of knowledge is very difficult. Division of knowledge into concept and assent. The problem resulting from the statement that concept pertains to everything, even to assent, and the solution of this problem. Their unity. Another problem: if we conceive the substantial or quantitative reality, then with the scientific reality, which is of the category of 'quality', it follows that a single thing falls under two categories, and the solution of this problem. The opposition between the intuitive and the acquired is either contrariety or privation and possession, and the investigations related to that. Division of each of concept and assent into necessary and theoretical. Not every concept and assent is necessary or theoretical, and proof of this claim with its evidence, along with mention of objections and their answers. Explanation of the invalidity of infinite regress. The proof of application in refuting infinite regress. The proof of correlation in refuting infinite regress. Definition of consideration and thought. The form that says seeking is impossible, and the response to it. Statement of the need for logic and its definition by description. Names of books and sciences: are they proper nouns, or common nouns, or generic names? The subject matter of logic: secondary intelligibles in terms of leading to an unknown concept or assent. The four main issues: what, which, whether, and why, and the places of use of each. Arrangement of issues with each other. First section: Concepts. It is impossible to judge the absolutely unknown, and the problem raised in that and its solution. Utterances: a chapter on indication and what pertains to it. A section on the reason for people's need for indication, and the division of indication into conventional and non-conventional, and verbal and non-verbal. Utterances are placed for meanings in themselves, not for mental or external images.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents of Bahr Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum. It lists the major sections and topics covered in the book. The list begins with the second section (al-tahqiq), then mentions the text of Sullam al-Ulum and the commentary itself. It proceeds through the introduction, virtues of the Prophet, attributes of God, then the introduction to logic covering the need for logic, definition of knowledge, division into tasawwur and tasdiq, various problems and solutions, opposition between intuitive and acquired knowledge, infinite regress proofs, definition of nazar and fikr, the form that seeking is impossible, etc. It then moves to the subject matter of logic (secondary intelligibles), the four main issues (ma, ayyu, hal, lima), and the first section on concepts including indication and utterances. This table of contents provides a roadmap of the entire work, showing its logical structure from epistemology to the nature of concepts and propositions.

Introduction and Front Matter

Understanding Tasawwur and Tasdiq from the Table of Contents
Table of contents of Bahr Ulum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur and tasdiq as the two fundamental divisions of knowledge in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
All logical inquiry begins with the split between concept and assent.

The table of contents introduces two crucial terms: tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). These are the building blocks of all knowledge. In this lesson, we will understand what they mean and why they are important.

Guided Path
1
Locate the terms in the table of contents
Find 'تقسيم العلم إلى تصور وتصديق' in the passage. This shows that knowledge is divided into concept and assent.
2
Define tasawwur
Tasawwur is a simple apprehension of something without judging it true or false. For example, having the concept of 'human' without affirming existence.
Tasawwur is the first type of knowledge.
3
Define tasdiq
Tasdiq is an assent or judgment, such as affirming or negating something. For example, believing 'Humans exist' is a tasdiq.
Tasdiq involves a claim of truth.
4
Understand their relationship
Tasawwur is prior; we must have concepts before we can make judgments about them. The table of contents shows that the book treats tasawwur first in the first section.

Index of book contents. Subject: Second section: Verification. Text of Sullam al-Ulum. Commentary of Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Wusul. Introduction of the book. Some virtues of the Chosen One, may God bless him and grant him peace. Investigations related to some attributes of transcendence mentioned in the author's speech. People's schools in the attribute of God's knowledge. Investigations related to the act of making. Introduction to the science of logic, including the statement of the need for logic, leading to its definition by description: knowledge is intuitive, not known. Refining the reality of knowledge is very difficult. Division of knowledge into concept and assent. The problem resulting from the statement that concept pertains to everything, even to assent, and the solution of this problem. Their unity. Another problem: if we conceive the substantial or quantitative reality, then with the scientific reality, which is of the category of 'quality', it follows that a single thing falls under two categories, and the solution of this problem. The opposition between the intuitive and the acquired is either contrariety or privation and possession, and the investigations related to that. Division of each of concept and assent into necessary and theoretical. Not every concept and assent is necessary or theoretical, and proof of this claim with its evidence, along with mention of objections and their answers. Explanation of the invalidity of infinite regress. The proof of application in refuting infinite regress. The proof of correlation in refuting infinite regress. Definition of consideration and thought. The form that says seeking is impossible, and the response to it. Statement of the need for logic and its definition by description. Names of books and sciences: are they proper nouns, or common nouns, or generic names? The subject matter of logic: secondary intelligibles in terms of leading to an unknown concept or assent. The four main issues: what, which, whether, and why, and the places of use of each. Arrangement of issues with each other. First section: Concepts. It is impossible to judge the absolutely unknown, and the problem raised in that and its solution. Utterances: a chapter on indication and what pertains to it. A section on the reason for people's need for indication, and the division of indication into conventional and non-conventional, and verbal and non-verbal. Utterances are placed for meanings in themselves, not for mental or external images.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate

What Logic Studies: Secondary Intelligibles
Table of contents of Bahr Ulum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic as secondary intelligibles (ma'qulat thanawiyya) and explain why logic does not study primary intelligibles.

Core Idea
Logic studies the tools of reasoning, not the objects themselves.

The table of contents tells us that the subject matter of logic is 'secondary intelligibles' (al-ma'qulat al-thanawiyya). This is a key concept. In this lesson, we will understand what secondary intelligibles are and why they are appropriate for logic.

Guided Path
1
Locate the phrase in the table of contents
Find 'موضوع علم المنطق المعقولات الثانية' in the passage. This states that the subject of logic is secondary intelligibles.
2
Distinguish primary from secondary intelligibles
Primary intelligibles are concepts of external things, like 'human' or 'tree'. Secondary intelligibles are concepts that apply to other concepts, like 'universal', 'particular', 'subject', 'predicate'.
Secondary intelligibles are tools for analyzing thought.
3
Understand why they are the subject
Logic is concerned with how we reason from known to unknown. To do that, we need to examine the forms of reasoning, which involve secondary intelligibles like 'premise', 'conclusion', 'syllogism'.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Subject Matter of Logic: Secondary Intelligibles
Defines the subject matter of logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject matter of logic and distinguish it from the subject matter of other Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Logic studies secondary intelligibles, not the external world—it is the tool of reasoning.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of logic as given in the table of contents of Sharh Bahr al-Ulum. Understanding the subject matter is crucial because it determines what logic can and cannot prove, and why it is a distinct science.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find the line in the table of contents that states the subject of logic: 'موضوع علم المنطق المعقولات الثانية من حيث الإيصال إلى مجهول تصوري او تصديقي'. This is the basis of the lesson.
This definition appears in the context of listing the foundations of logic.
2
Analyze the key phrase 'secondary intelligibles'
Secondary intelligibles (المعقولات الثانية) are concepts that apply to other concepts, e.g., 'generality', 'specificity', 'necessity'. They are not direct representations of external objects.
In contrast, primary intelligibles are concepts like 'man' or 'horse' that directly signify external entities.
3
Connect to the purpose of logic
The phrase 'from the perspective of leading to an unknown conception or assent' shows that logic is a methodological tool. It does not study secondary intelligibles in themselves, but as instruments for acquiring new knowledge.
This aligns with the definition of logic as an instrumental science (آلة).
4
Compare with other Islamic sciences
Unlike kalam (which studies divine attributes), fiqh (which studies legal rulings), or physics, logic studies the formal principles of reasoning. A mutakallim uses logic, but the subject matter of logic is not the same as that of kalam.
For example, the concept 'possible' is a secondary intelligible studied in logic, but its application to the world is in kalam.

تعريف الاسم، ومن خواصه الحكم عليه
تقسيم المفرد المتحد المعنى إلى جزئي ومتواط ومشكك الضمائر وأسماء الإشارة جزئية ، وتحقيق ذلك
الاختلافات بين المشائية والإشراقية في هل يوجد التشكيك في الماهيات

تقسيم المفرد الكثير المعنى إلى مشترك ومنقول وحقيقة ومجاز، وتعريف الكل
أنواع المجاز حسب تنوع علاقاته
Not every conception and assent is necessary or theoretical, and proving this claim with its proofs, along with mentioning what objections arise and their answers. Statement of the invalidity of infinite regress. The proof of correlation in refuting infinite regress. The proof of reciprocity in refuting infinite regress. Definition of speculation and thought. The argument that seeking is impossible, and the answer to it. Statement of the need for logic and its definition by description. Whether names of books and sciences are personal proper names, or generic proper names, or generic names. The subject matter of logic is the secondary intelligibles from the perspective of leading to an unknown conception or assent. The four mothers of inquiries: what, which, whether, and why, and the uses of each. The order of inquiries with respect to each other. The first section: conceptions. It is impossible to judge the absolute unknown, and the problem that arises in that and its solution. Chapter on indication and what relates to it: section on the reason for people’s need for indication, and division of indication into conventional and non-conventional, verbal and non-verbal. Words are placed for meanings as they are in themselves, not for mental or external forms. Division of indication into conformity, inclusion, and implication. The author’s choice of the Arab school over the logicians in defining implicative indication. The statement of Imam al-Razi that implication is abandoned in the sciences, and the author’s refutation of him. Statement of the relations of the three indications to each other. Chapter on simplicity and composition. Definition of simple and compound. Division of the simple into particle, verb, and noun. The existential words from the particle. Definition of the verb. Not every verb in Arabic is a verb according to the logicians. Definition of the noun, and among its properties is being judged upon. Division of the simple with one meaning into particular, univocal, and equivocal; pronouns and demonstratives are particular, and verification of that. The differences between the Peripatetics and Illuminationists regarding whether equivocation exists in essences. There is no equivocation in essences nor in accidents, but rather in the attribution of individuals to them. Division of the simple with multiple meanings into homonym, transmitted, literal, and metaphorical, and definition of each. Types of metaphor according to the diversity of its relations.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents of the commentary 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum' on the logical poem 'Sullam al-Ulum'. It lists the main topics covered in the book, organized in the order they appear. The topics include fundamental discussions in logic: the distinction between conception and assent, the need for logic, definitions, the subject matter of logic (secondary intelligibles), the four types of inquiries (what, which, whether, why), and sections on indication (verbal and non-verbal, conventional and non-conventional, and the three types: conformity, inclusion, and implication). It also covers the debate on implicative indication, al-Razi's view, and the author's response. Then it moves to topics in words: simple vs. compound, particles, verbs, nouns, and divisions of simple terms (particular, univocal, equivocal, homonym, transmitted, literal, metaphorical). The table of contents shows that the book covers the foundational elements of logic as typically taught in the Islamic tradition, with a focus on concepts and terms.

منطق
معقولات ثانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this table of contents, what is the subject matter of logic?
A) Natural objects
B) Secondary intelligibles from the perspective of leading to an unknown
C) Primary intelligibles
D) Language and grammar

Key Takeaway
Logic's subject matter is secondary intelligibles proper to it alone, focusing on their role in guiding the mind from known to unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept 'obligation' (وجوب) as used in usul al-fiqh. Explain whether it is a primary or secondary intelligible, and why a jurist needs logic to reason with it.
?
Identify which of the following is a secondary intelligible: 'horse', 'generality', 'sun', 'contingency'. Justify your answer using the definition from the lesson.

Introduction and Front Matter

The Division of Indication (Dalaalat)
Classifies types of indication and defends implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify different types of indication (dalala) and identify which type is at play in a given Islamic text.

Core Idea
Indication is the bridge between words and meanings; its classification is essential for correct interpretation of revelation and legal texts.

This lesson explores the division of indication as outlined in the table of contents. The classification into conformity, inclusion, and implication is crucial in usul al-fiqh for understanding how divine commands convey meaning. The author also defends implicative indication against al-Razi's criticism.

Guided Path
1
Recall the primary divisions of indication
From the table of contents: indication is first divided into conventional (وضعي) and non-conventional, and verbal (لفظي) and non-verbal. Then the three subtypes: conformity (مطابقية), inclusion (تضمنية), and implication (التزامية).
These divisions are based on the relationship between the word and what it signifies.
2
Understand conformity and inclusion
Conformity is when the word indicates the whole meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates the entire house). Inclusion is when it indicates a part of the meaning (e.g., 'house' indicates a room).
These are straightforward and accepted by all.
3
Focus on implicative indication
Implication is when the word indicates something external to the meaning that necessarily follows from it (e.g., 'house' indicates the inhabitants). The table of contents notes al-Razi's view that this type is abandoned in sciences, but the author refutes him.
The author sides with the Arab school, which accepts implication.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the Quranic verse 'The heavens and the earth were joined together' (21:30). By conformity, it indicates the original unity. By inclusion, it indicates components. By implication, it indicates the power of Allah who separated them.
Implication is often used in theological arguments.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents of the commentary 'Sharh Bahr al-Ulum' on the logical poem 'Sullam al-Ulum'. It lists the main topics covered in the book, organized in the order they appear. The topics include fundamental discussions in logic: the distinction between conception and assent, the need for logic, definitions, the subject matter of logic (secondary intelligibles), the four types of inquiries (what, which, whether, why), and sections on indication (verbal and non-verbal, conventional and non-conventional, and the three types: conformity, inclusion, and implication). It also covers the debate on implicative indication, al-Razi's view, and the author's response. Then it moves to topics in words: simple vs. compound, particles, verbs, nouns, and divisions of simple terms (particular, univocal, equivocal, homonym, transmitted, literal, metaphorical). The table of contents shows that the book covers the foundational elements of logic as typically taught in the Islamic tradition, with a focus on concepts and terms.

Division of Simple and Compound Terms
Classifies terms into simple/compound and subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Arabic terms as simple or compound, and within simple terms, distinguish between particular, univocal, and equivocal.

Core Idea
The logician's classification of words is a tool for precise definition and avoiding equivocation in theological and legal discourse.

This lesson covers the division of terms from the table of contents. Understanding whether a term is particular or univocal affects how we apply rulings in fiqh and kalam. The author also discusses the debate on equivocation (tashkik) between Peripatetics and Illuminationists.

Guided Path
1
Define simple and compound
A simple term (مفرد) is a single word like 'كتاب' (book). A compound term (مركب) is a phrase like 'كتاب زيد' (Zaid's book). The table of contents begins this section with their definitions.
This distinction is important for analyzing definitions and arguments.
2
Classify simple terms into three parts
Simple terms are divided into particle (أداة), verb (كلمة), and noun (اسم). The table of contents notes that not every Arabic verb is considered a verb by logicians (e.g., non-temporal verbs).
This classification differs from Arabic grammar; logicians focus on meaning.
3
Understand the division of simple terms with one meaning
Such terms are either particular (جزئي) like 'Zaid', univocal (متواطئ) like 'man', or equivocal (مشكك) like 'existence'. The table of contents clarifies that equivocation is not in the essence but in the attribution of individuals.
The Peripatetic-Illuminationist debate on equivocation in essences is referenced.
4
Apply to Islamic example
The term 'light' (نور) applied to God and to the sun is equivocal: both are called light, but in different degrees. Understanding this prevents anthropomorphism in kalam.
Similarly, 'existence' (وجود) is equivocal between God and creatures.

Introduction and Front Matter

Navigating Bahr al-Ulum: Using the Table of Contents
Table of contents listing topics and page numbers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the table of contents from Bahr al-Ulum to locate key topics and trace the logical progression of the commentary.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a roadmap that reveals the logical order before you read.

When you open a classical logic commentary like Bahr al-Ulum, the first thing you see is often a table of contents (fihris). This is not just an index—it is a bird's-eye view of the entire argumentative structure. By learning to read it skillfully, you can navigate directly to the discussions you need and see how topics build on each other. This lesson will teach you to parse the table of contents from pages 624-625 of Bahr al-Ulum.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the layout
Notice that the table has two columns: one for page numbers and one for subjects. The page numbers are on the left, but many are corrupted by OCR errors. Ignore the corrupt digits and focus on the topic titles.
The original page numbers help locate discussions in the printed book.
2
Identify major sections
Scan for large headings: 'تقسيم الدلالة' (Division of signification), 'مبحث الإفراد والتركيب' (Topic of single and compound), 'فصل في الكليات الخمس' (Chapter on the five universals). These are the main parts of the commentary.
The progression from signification to terms to universals follows the standard order of logic texts.
3
Trace the subtopics
Under each major heading, look for indented subentries. For example, under 'مبحث الإفراد والتركيب' you see definitions of single/compound, division of single into particle/verb/noun, etc.
Subtopics show the detailed logical breakdown of each concept.
4
Note key terms and debates
Spot important terms like 'الدلالة' and 'الكليات الخمس' and debates like 'قول الإمام الرازي بأن الالتزام مهجور' (al-Razi's saying about iltizam). These mark crucial points in the commentary.
Use these entries to jump to specific controversies or definitions.
5
Use the table for navigation
When studying a particular topic, find its page number (if reliable) and turn to that page. If the page number is corrupted, use the surrounding entries to narrow the search.
The table is a practical tool for efficient reading, not a substitute for the text itself.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents (fihris) from the commentary Bahr al-Ulum on Sullam al-Ulum. It lists the main sections and subsections covered in the book, along with corresponding page numbers. The topics range from introductory discussions on signification (dilalah) and its divisions to more advanced topics like the five universals (al-kulliyyat al-khamsah). The student can use this index to navigate the commentary and see the logical progression of topics. The table of contents reflects the structure of a typical work on logic (mantiq), starting with terms (alfaz) and their classifications, moving to propositions (qadaya), and culminating in the five arts (sana'at khams). The page numbers, though partially corrupted by OCR, show the extensive coverage of the commentary. This is a front_matter passage meant to orient the reader, not to convey a logical argument.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Logical Roadmap of Bahr al-Ulum
Lists the topics and sections of the logical portion of the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the sequence of topics in Bahr al-Ulum's logic section and explain how each major section prepares for the next.

Core Idea
The table of contents mirrors the logical order: language → concept → universal → definition.

This lesson unpacks the table of contents (fihris) of Bahr al-Ulum's logic portion. Rather than a mere list, it is a carefully ordered curriculum that moves from linguistic foundations (metaphor, literal meaning, synonymy) through the division of concepts and relations among universals, to the five universals and their detailed investigation. Understanding this flow helps you locate any topic quickly and appreciate how each part builds on the earlier ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main phases
The table of contents has three broad phases: (1) linguistic preliminaries (majaz, haqiqah, taraduf, khabar, insha'), (2) conceptualization and universals (concept division, particular/universal, relations, contradiction), (3) the five universals with their investigations and related issues. Scan the list to see these clusters.
2
Locate the five universals chapter
Find the heading 'فصل في الكليات الخمس' (Chapter on the five universals). Notice it appears after the discussion of contradictories and before the detailed investigations of genus, species, etc. This placement shows that the five universals are the culmination of the conceptual groundwork.
3
Trace the order within the five universals
After the chapter heading, the table lists five investigations: genus, species, difference, property, general accident. Each has sub‑topics (definitions, problems, solutions). This order is not arbitrary; it follows the logical priority of essential over accidental universals.
4
Note the concluding section
The final entry is a conclusion discussing the logical, natural, and mental universal. This serves as a capstone, linking the abstract discussion of universals to their modes of existence in the mind and in reality.

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents (fihris) of the logical section of Bahr al-Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum. It lists the topics in the order they appear in the book, starting with linguistic issues such as metaphor (majaz), literal meaning (haqiqah), synonymy (taraduf), and the division of sentences into complete and incomplete. Then it moves to the core of logic: conceptualization, division of concepts into particular and universal, relations among universals, contradiction, and the five universals. Detailed investigations follow on genus (jins), species (naw'), difference (fasl), property (khassah), and general accident ('arad 'amm). The table concludes with a discussion of the logical, natural, and mental universal. This fihris shows the systematic structure of the work, typical of post-classical Islamic logic textbooks.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner
10 min
The Five Universals: Core of Definition
Lists the five universals and their detailed investigations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five universals in their proper order and identify which are essential and which are accidental.

Core Idea
The five universals are the backbone of every definition; each plays a distinct role in analyzing concepts.

The table of contents highlights the 'Chapter on the five universals' (فصل في الكليات الخمس) as the heart of the logic section. It then details five investigations: genus, species, difference, property, and general accident. This lesson helps you commit these five to memory and understand why they are essential for constructing and evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the five universals in order
From the table, extract the five headings: المبحث الأول: الجنس (genus), المبحث الثاني: النوع (species), المبحث الثالث: الفصل (difference), المبحث الرابع: الخاصة (property), المبحث الخامس: العرض العام (general accident). Write them down in that order.
The order is significant: essential universals (genus, species, difference) come before accidental ones (property, general accident).
2
Identify essential vs. accidental
Categorize: genus, species, and difference are essential (they form the quiddity); property and general accident are accidental (they do not enter the definition of the essence). This is a standard classification in Islamic logic.
The term 'essential' here means 'part of the definition', not 'existing necessarily'.
3
Match each universal to its function
In a definition (hadd), genus gives the broad category, difference specifies the unique characteristic, and species is the combination. Property is a non‑essential concomitant, and general accident is a non‑essential attribute that applies to other species as well. Check the sub‑topics for examples (e.g., 'الفرق بين الجنس والمادة').
4
Relate to the conclusion
The concluding section on logical, natural, and mental universal shows how the same universal can be considered in three ways. This connects the purely logical discussion to ontology and epistemology.

Introduction and Front Matter
beginner

Navigating the Table of Contents of Bahr al-Ulum
Table of contents listing topics and page numbers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major sections of Bahr al-Ulum from its table of contents and locate topics related to the five universals, definitions, and assents.

Core Idea
A table of contents is a logical map; mastering it reveals the architecture of a text.

This lesson walks you through the table of contents of Bahr al-Ulum, a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum. Understanding this map will help you navigate the text and see how the author organizes the science of logic into two main parts: tasawwurat (conceptions) and tasdiqat (assents).

Guided Path
1
Identify major headings
Scan the table of contents for large headers like 'المبحث الأول' (first section) and 'القسم الثاني' (second section). These indicate the main divisions of the book.
2
Distinguish the two main parts
Notice that the first part covers conceptions (tasawwurat) with discussions on the five universals and definitions. The second part covers assents (tasdiqat), starting with 'القسم الثاني التصديقات'.
This division mirrors the standard structure of logic textbooks.
3
Locate the five universals
Within the first part, find entries for each of the five universals: جنس (genus), نوع (species), فصل (differentia), خاصة (property), عرض عام (general accident). Note sub-issues like 'إشكالية أن الكلي جنس الخمسة'.
The TOC also includes a conclusion (خاتمة) on the logical, natural, and mental universal.
4
Find the definitions section
After the five universals, look for 'فصل في المعرف' (Chapter on definition) and its sub-topics like conditions, divisions, and doubts.
This is a separate major subsection within tasawwurat.
5
Note the assents section
Towards the end, locate 'القسم الثاني التصديقات' and its first topic: the difference between summary and detailed judgment.
This section is shorter in the TOC, indicating it may be less detailed or the TOC is truncated.

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the book Bahr Ulum, listing the topics covered and their page numbers. It begins with the main heading 'Subject' followed by 'Table of Contents of the Book' and 'Page'. The entries are organized by section, starting with discussions on the five universals (al-kulli), then moving to genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), property (khassa), and general accident (arad 'amm). Each section includes sub-issues and problems. The second part of the table covers the section on definitions (al-mu'arraf) and its conditions, followed by the second major section on assents (tasdiqat). The table also includes page numbers for each topic. This provides an overview of the book's structure, which is a commentary on Sullam al-Ulum, a standard text in Islamic logic.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
5 min
The Logical Architecture of Bahr Ulum
Opening overview of the book's sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two-part structure of a classical logic text and explain why definitions precede assents.

Core Idea
Definitions are the foundation; assents are the superstructure.

This lesson maps the logical journey from concepts to judgments as outlined in the table of contents of Bahr Ulum. Understanding the overall structure helps you see how the book builds from clarifying terms (tasawwur) to establishing truths (tasdiq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main sections
From the passage, locate the two major divisions: 'Chapter on Definition' and 'The second section: Assents'.
These correspond to the two cognitive goals: conceptualization and assertion.
2
Notice the order
Observe that definitions come first, then assents. This reflects the logical priority: you must define terms before making judgments about them.
3
List sub-topics under definitions
The definition section includes conditions, divisions, refutation of al-Razi's doubt, and the verbal definition debate. These are foundational issues.
These sub-topics show that the author is not just presenting a simple definition but engaging with critiques.
4
List sub-topics under assents
Under assents, the table mentions judgment types, parts of a proposition, the copula, and division of propositions. This builds on the clarified concepts.

Source Text

فصل في المعرف. تعريف المعرف، وشروطه، وأقسامه. ما يُحد ويُحد به، وما لا يُحد ولا يُحد به. بعض الأبحاث المتعلقة بالحد. دفع شك الإمام الرازي أن التعريف تحصيل الحاصل. الاختلاف الواقع بين السيد والسعد في أن التعريف اللفظي هل هو من المطالب التصورية، أم من المطالب التصديقية. لا يتوجه على التعريف شيء من المُنوع، وأما ما تضمنه من الأحكام الضمنية فيجوز إيرادها عليه، إلا أن العلماء منع ذلك أيضا. يجوز نقض الحد بإبطال الطرد والعكس. تجري المعارضة في الحدود الحقيقية، دون الرسمية. اللفظ المفرد لا يدل على التفصيل، وما يترتب على ذلك من الأبحاث. القسم الثاني التصديقات. الفرق بين الحكم الإجمالي والحكم التفصيلي، والنسبة تدخل في متعلق الحكم. أجزاء القضية ثلاثة، وما يتعلق به من الأبحاث. وفي لغة العرب رابطة كغيرها من اللغات ولا حاجة إلى الاستعارة خلافا للسعد. ما يراه المحقق صوابا في هذه المسألة. تقسيم القضية إلى الحملية والشرطية. هل الحكم في الشرطية في الجزاء، أم باللزوم بين التالي والمقدم، وهل بين النحاة والمناطقة خلاف في ذلك؟ فصل، تقسيم القضية باعتبار الموضوع إلى شخصية ومهملة وطبيعية ومحصورة. المهملة تلازم الجزئية عند المتأخرين. اختيار قول الدواني إن الحكم في المحصورة على نفس الحقيقة، والجزئيات معلومة بالعرض. المحصورات أربع، وسور كل منها. تبصرة في تحقيق المحصورات. تعبيرهم عن الموضوع بـ (ج) وعن المحمول بـ(ب). الإطلاقات الثلاثة للفظ «الكل». المعتبر في العلوم من تلك الإطلاقات هو «الكل الأفرادي». المراد بـ(ج) هو ذات الموضوع، أي ما يصدق عليه الموضوع من الأفراد. الخلاف بين الفارابي وابن سينا في معنى صدق عنوان الموضوع على ذاته. عقد الوضع وعقد الحمل (المحقق).
Chapter on Definition. Definition of definition, its conditions, and its divisions. What is defined and what is defined by, and what is not defined nor defines by. Some investigations related to definition. Refutation of the doubt of Imam al-Razi that definition is the acquisition of what is already acquired. The disagreement between al-Sayyid and al-Sa'd regarding whether verbal definition is among conceptual demands or among assertive demands. Nothing from the objections applies to definition; as for what it includes of implicit judgments, it is permissible to object to them, except that the scholars also forbade that. It is permissible to refute the definition by invalidating the 'tard' and 'aks'. Objection is valid against real definitions, not against descriptive ones. The single word does not indicate detail, and the investigations resulting from that. The second section: Assents. The difference between total judgment and detailed judgment, and the relation enters into the object of judgment. The parts of a proposition are three, and the investigations related to it. In the language of the Arabs, there is a copula like other languages, and there is no need for metaphor, contrary to al-Sa'd. What the investigator deems correct in this issue. Division of proposition into categorical and conditional. Is the judgment in the conditional upon the consequent, or upon the implication between the consequent and antecedent? Is there a disagreement between grammarians and logicians on that? Chapter: Division of proposition with respect to subject into individual, indefinite, natural, and quantified. The indefinite implies the particular according to the later scholars. Choosing the view of al-Dawani that the judgment in a quantified proposition is upon the essence itself, and the particulars are known accidentally. The quantified propositions are four, and the quantifier of each. Insight into the verification of quantified propositions. Their expression of the subject by (J) and of the predicate by (B). The three usages of the word 'all'. What is considered in sciences among those usages is 'the individual all'. What is meant by (J) is the essence of the subject, i.e., what the subject applies to of individuals. The disagreement between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina on the meaning of the predicate of the subject's title upon itself. The contract of subject and contract of predicate (al-Muhaqqiq).
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the treatise 'Bahr Ulum' on Islamic logic. It outlines the major sections, starting with definitions and their conditions, then moves to the second major section on assents (tasdiqat). Under definitions, the author discusses the refutation of al-Razi's doubt, the disagreement on verbal definition, and the permissibility of objections. For assents, the table covers the parts of a proposition, the copula debate, the division of propositions into categorical and conditional, and the division by subject type. It also notes controversies among later scholars such as al-Dawani, al-Sayyid, and al-Sa'd, and references disagreements between al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. This table serves as a roadmap, showing the logical flow from conceptual to assertive matters, with focus on definition, proposition structure, and quantified propositions.

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
7 min
Key Debates on Verbal Definition
Debates within the definition chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two key debates on definition and explain why verbal definition is considered a conceptual demand.

Core Idea
Verbal definition is not merely explaining words; it is a conceptual tool.

The definition chapter in Bahr Ulum is not straightforward; it contains major philosophical disagreements. This lesson focuses on the refutation of Imam al-Razi's skepticism and the debate over whether verbal definition is conceptual or assertive.

Guided Path
1
Locate al-Razi's doubt
In the passage, find the phrase 'دفع شك الإمام الرازي أن التعريف تحصيل الحاصل'. This is the author's refutation of al-Razi's claim that definition is acquiring what is already acquired.
Al-Razi's doubt challenges the very utility of definition.
2
Restate the doubt in your own words
If you already know what a thing is, defining it adds nothing; if you don't, you cannot define it. So definition seems impossible or useless.
This is a classic epistemological problem.
3
Find the verbal definition debate
Look for 'الاختلاف الواقع بين السيد والسعد في أن التعريف اللفظي هل هو من المطالب التصورية، أم من المطالب التصديقية'. This records a dispute between al-Sayyid and al-Sa'd.
Al-Sayyid and al-Sa'd are later commentators (al-Jurjani and al-Taftazani).
4
Understand the distinction
Conceptual demands (tasawwuri) are about grasping meaning; assertive demands (tasdiqi) are about affirming truth. Is defining a word a matter of understanding or of asserting a relation?
This classification affects how we treat definitions in science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Introduction and Front Matter
intermediate
6 min
The Copula in Arabic: A Grammatical and Logical Dispute
The copula in Arabic and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to argue whether Arabic has an explicit copula and explain why the issue matters for logical analysis.

Core Idea
Whether Arabic has a copula affects how we formalize propositions.

The table of contents highlights a debate: does Arabic have a copula like other languages? This lesson examines the claim that Arabic does not need to borrow a copula, contrary to al-Sa'd's view.

Guided Path
1
Find the three parts of a proposition
The passage states 'أجزاء القضية ثلاثة' (the parts of a proposition are three). Typically: subject, predicate, and copula.
The third part is the copula, which links subject and predicate.
2
Identify the claim about Arabic
Look for 'وفي لغة العرب رابطة كغيرها من اللغات' – Arabic has a copula like other languages.
This opposes the view that Arabic lacks an explicit copula and must use metaphor.
3
Note the dissenter
The phrase 'خلافا للسعد' indicates al-Sa'd (al-Taftazani) disagreed, arguing Arabic has no copula.
Al-Sa'd is a major commentator.
4
Understand the implication
If Arabic has no copula, then nominal sentences like 'Zayd is standing' would lack a logical link, making formalization difficult. The author sides with those who see an implicit copula.
This affects how propositions are quantified and used in syllogisms.
